### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single house is a building. A few schools are classified as buildings. As a result, not all schools are houses.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Every single house is a building. A few schools are classified as buildings. As a result, not all schools are houses.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let H = houses
    *   Let B = buildings
    *   Let S = schools

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** Every single house is a building.
        *   ∀x (H(x) → B(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** A few schools are classified as buildings.
        *   ∃x (S(x) ∧ B(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** As a result, not all schools are houses.
        *   ¬∀x (S(x) → H(x))
        *   (This is logically equivalent to ∃x (S(x) ∧ ¬H(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
The argument is invalid. The premises state that all houses are buildings, and some schools are buildings. This does not necessarily mean that some schools are *not* houses. It is possible that *all* schools are houses (and thus also buildings), which would make the conclusion ("not all schools are houses") false, while the premises remain true.

Consider a scenario where:
*   All houses are buildings.
*   All schools are houses. (This makes the conclusion false)
*   Therefore, all schools are buildings. (This satisfies Premise 2, as "some schools are buildings" would be true if "all schools are buildings" is true, assuming there's at least one school).

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the argument is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no mythical creatures who are people. A few mythical creatures are aliens. It is the case that a portion of all aliens are people.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no mythical creatures who are people. A few mythical creatures are aliens. It is the case that a portion of all aliens are people.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mythical creature
    *   Let P(x) = x is a person
    *   Let A(x) = x is an alien

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There are no mythical creatures who are people.
        *   ∀x (M(x) → ¬P(x))  (or equivalently, ¬∃x (M(x) ∧ P(x)))
    *   Premise 2: A few mythical creatures are aliens.
        *   ∃x (M(x) ∧ A(x))
    *   Conclusion: It is the case that a portion of all aliens are people.
        *   ∃x (A(x) ∧ P(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
Let's assume the premises are true and see if the conclusion *must* follow.

*   **Premise 1 (True):** No mythical creatures are people. This means the set of mythical creatures (M) and the set of people (P) are entirely separate; they have no members in common.
*   **Premise 2 (True):** Some mythical creatures are aliens. This means there is at least one individual that is both a mythical creature and an alien. Let's call this individual 'k'. So, k is M and k is A.

Now, consider the conclusion: Some aliens are people.

It is possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false.
Imagine a scenario:
*   **M = {Unicorn, Dragon}** (mythical creatures)
*   **P = {John, Mary}** (people)
*   **A = {Unicorn, Martian}** (aliens)

Let's check the premises with this scenario:
*   **Premise 1:** Are there no mythical creatures who are people? Yes, {Unicorn, Dragon} and {John, Mary} have no overlap. (TRUE)
*   **Premise 2:** Are a few mythical creatures aliens? Yes, 'Unicorn' is both a mythical creature and an alien. (TRUE)

Now, let's check the conclusion:
*   **Conclusion:** Are a portion of all aliens people? This means, is there any overlap between A = {Unicorn, Martian} and P = {John, Mary}? No, there is no individual that is both an alien and a person in this scenario. So, the conclusion ∃x (A(x) ∧ P(x)) is FALSE.

Since we found a situation where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**. The existence of mythical creatures who are aliens does not force any alien to be a person, given that mythical creatures and people are mutually exclusive.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Liquids are in no way substances. Anything that is a liquid is a fluid. It's the case that some fluids are not substances.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let L(x) = "x is a liquid"
    *   Let S(x) = "x is a substance"
    *   Let F(x) = "x is a fluid"

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "Liquids are in no way substances."
        *   ∀x (L(x) → ¬S(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Anything that is a liquid is a fluid."
        *   ∀x (L(x) → F(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "It's the case that some fluids are not substances."
        *   ∃x (F(x) ∧ ¬S(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation for Invalidity:**
This syllogism is invalid because it attempts to derive an existential conclusion (∃x ...) from two universal premises (∀x ...). In modern predicate logic, universal statements do not imply the existence of any members in the categories they describe.

Consider a scenario where there are no liquids (i.e., the set L is empty).
*   **Premise 1:** If there are no liquids, then "∀x (L(x) → ¬S(x))" is vacuously true (because L(x) is always false, making the implication true).
*   **Premise 2:** Similarly, if there are no liquids, then "∀x (L(x) → F(x))" is also vacuously true.

Now, let's consider the conclusion: "∃x (F(x) ∧ ¬S(x))".
If there are no liquids, the premises are true. However, the conclusion states that there exists *some* fluid that is not a substance. This does not necessarily follow. For example, imagine a universe where there are no liquids, and all existing fluids *are* substances. In such a case, the premises would be true, but the conclusion would be false.

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no animals that are living creatures. Every single mammal is a living creature. Consequently, there exist some mammals which are not animals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no animals that are living creatures. Every single mammal is a living creature. Consequently, there exist some mammals which are not animals.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = animals
    *   Let B = living creatures
    *   Let C = mammals

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There are no animals that are living creatures."
        *   This means for all x, if x is an animal, then x is not a living creature.
        *   Symbolically: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Every single mammal is a living creature."
        *   This means for all x, if x is a mammal, then x is a living creature.
        *   Symbolically: ∀x (C(x) → B(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "Consequently, there exist some mammals which are not animals."
        *   This means there is at least one x such that x is a mammal and x is not an animal.
        *   Symbolically: ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**

Let's analyze the premises:
*   From Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   This means if something is an animal, it is not a living creature.
    *   The contrapositive of this statement is also true: ∀x (B(x) → ¬A(x)) (If something is a living creature, then it is not an animal).
*   From Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → B(x))
    *   This means if something is a mammal, it is a living creature.

Now, let's combine these:
If something is a mammal (C(x)), then it is a living creature (B(x)) by Premise 2.
If something is a living creature (B(x)), then it is not an animal (¬A(x)) by the contrapositive of Premise 1.
Therefore, if something is a mammal (C(x)), then it is not an animal (¬A(x)).
This leads to the derived universal statement: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x)) (All mammals are not animals).

The conclusion of the syllogism is ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x)) (Some mammals are not animals).

In modern symbolic logic, a universal statement (like "All mammals are not animals") does not necessarily imply an existential statement (like "Some mammals are not animals"). The existential conclusion only follows if we assume that the category "mammals" (C) is not empty. The premises themselves do not guarantee the existence of mammals.

Consider a scenario where the set of mammals (C) is empty:
*   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x)) could be true (e.g., if A = {dogs}, B = {cats}, then no dogs are cats).
*   Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → B(x)) would be vacuously true if C is empty (e.g., if C = {unicorns}, B = {cats}, then all unicorns are cats).
*   Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x)) would be false if C is empty (there are no unicorns that are not dogs).

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no fish which are mammals. No lions are considered fish. Some lions are not mammals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no fish which are mammals. No lions are considered fish. Some lions are not mammals.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let F = fish
    Let M = mammals
    Let L = lions

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x)) (There are no fish which are mammals)
    Premise 2: ∀x (L(x) → ¬F(x)) (No lions are considered fish)
    Conclusion: ∃x (L(x) ∧ ¬M(x)) (Some lions are not mammals)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
To show the syllogism is invalid, we need to find a scenario where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false.

Let's assume the conclusion is false. If "Some lions are not mammals" is false, then its negation must be true: "All lions are mammals" (i.e., ∀x (L(x) → M(x))).

Consider a world where:
*   There are no fish. (This makes Premise 1 and Premise 2 vacuously true for the 'fish' part).
*   All lions are mammals.

Let's define a domain with a single individual, "Leo":
*   L(Leo) = True (Leo is a lion)
*   M(Leo) = True (Leo is a mammal)
*   F(Leo) = False (Leo is not a fish)

Now, let's check the premises and conclusion:
*   **Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x))**
    For Leo: F(Leo) → ¬M(Leo) becomes False → ¬True, which is False → False, which is **True**. (Premise 1 holds)

*   **Premise 2: ∀x (L(x) → ¬F(x))**
    For Leo: L(Leo) → ¬F(Leo) becomes True → ¬False, which is True → True, which is **True**. (Premise 2 holds)

*   **Conclusion: ∃x (L(x) ∧ ¬M(x))**
    For Leo: L(Leo) ∧ ¬M(Leo) becomes True ∧ ¬True, which is True ∧ False, which is **False**. (The conclusion is false)

Since we found a model where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**. The premises only establish that lions and mammals are both distinct from fish; they do not necessitate any particular relationship (or lack thereof) between lions and mammals.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no books that are made of paper. There is not a single paperback that is a book. It follows, then, that some paperbacks are not made of paper.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no books that are made of paper. There is not a single paperback that is a book. It follows, then, that some paperbacks are not made of paper.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = books
    Let B = made of paper
    Let C = paperbacks

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))  (No books are made of paper)
    Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))  (No paperbacks are books)
    Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬B(x)) (Some paperbacks are not made of paper)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
To show invalidity, we need to find a scenario where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false.
If the conclusion "Some paperbacks are not made of paper" is false, then its negation must be true: "All paperbacks are made of paper" (∀x (C(x) → B(x))).

Consider the following interpretation:
*   Let A = fish
*   Let B = mammals
*   Let C = whales

Now, let's check the premises and the negated conclusion:
*   **Premise 1:** "No fish are mammals." (∀x (Fish(x) → ¬Mammal(x))) - This is **TRUE**.
*   **Premise 2:** "No whales are fish." (∀x (Whale(x) → ¬Fish(x))) - This is **TRUE**.
*   **Negated Conclusion:** "All whales are mammals." (∀x (Whale(x) → Mammal(x))) - This is also **TRUE**.

Since we found a scenario where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false (because its negation is true), the syllogism is **INVALID**. The premises do not logically guarantee the conclusion.

Anything that is a mammal is an animal. Every single dog is an animal. This means that all dogs are mammals.

2.  **Logical Formulation:**

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Nothing that is a mammal has feathers. A portion of the things that are birds have feathers. It must be the case that every single bird is not a mammal.

### Response:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let M(x) = x is a mammal
    Let F(x) = x has feathers
    Let B(x) = x is a bird

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (M(x) → ¬F(x)) (Nothing that is a mammal has feathers)
    Premise 2: ∃x (B(x) ∧ F(x)) (A portion of the things that are birds have feathers)
    Conclusion: ∀x (B(x) → ¬M(x)) (Every single bird is not a mammal)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    *   From Premise 1 (∀x (M(x) → ¬F(x))), we know that if something is a mammal, it does not have feathers. This is equivalent to saying that if something has feathers, it is not a mammal (∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x))).
    *   From Premise 2 (∃x (B(x) ∧ F(x))), we know there exists at least one individual, let's call it 'k', such that 'k' is a bird AND 'k' has feathers.
    *   Combining these, since 'k' has feathers (F(k)), and anything with feathers is not a mammal (F(x) → ¬M(x)), it follows that 'k' is not a mammal (¬M(k)).
    *   So, we can deduce: ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬M(x)) – "Some birds are not mammals."
    *   However, the conclusion states: ∀x (B(x) → ¬M(x)) – "All birds are not mammals" or "No birds are mammals."
    *   The fact that *some* birds are not mammals does not logically guarantee that *all* birds are not mammals. There could exist a bird that is also a mammal (and, by Premise 1, would therefore not have feathers).

    **Counterexample:**
    Consider a world where:
    *   **Mammals (M):** Dogs, Cats, Bats
    *   **Feathers (F):** Sparrows, Eagles
    *   **Birds (B):** Sparrows, Eagles, Bats

    *   **Premise 1: Nothing that is a mammal has feathers.**
        *   Dogs, Cats, Bats are mammals and none of them have feathers. (TRUE)
    *   **Premise 2: A portion of the things that are birds have feathers.**
        *   Sparrows and Eagles are birds and have feathers. (TRUE)
    *   **Conclusion: Every single bird is not a mammal.**
        *   Sparrows are birds and not mammals. (True)
        *   Eagles are birds and not mammals. (True)
        *   Bats are birds (in this example's definition of 'B') AND Bats *are* mammals. Therefore, the statement "Bats are not mammals" is FALSE.
        *   Since there is a bird (a bat) that *is* a mammal, the conclusion "Every single bird is not a mammal" is FALSE.

    Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is INVALID.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Each bird is a vertebrate. Each bird is an animal that has wings. So, some animals with wings must be vertebrates.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Each bird is a vertebrate. Each bird is an animal that has wings. So, some animals with wings must be vertebrates.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let B(x) = x is a bird
    *   Let V(x) = x is a vertebrate
    *   Let W(x) = x is an animal that has wings

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (B(x) → V(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → W(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (W(x) ∧ V(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
The premises state that every bird is a vertebrate and every bird is an animal that has wings. In natural language, the phrase "Each bird is..." typically implies that birds exist. If we assume there is at least one bird, then that bird must be both a vertebrate and an animal that has wings. Therefore, there exists at least one entity that is an animal with wings and is also a vertebrate, which is precisely what the conclusion states.

    *   Conclusion: ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬P(x))   (There exists an x that is a triangle and not a polygon)

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:

There is no overlap between the category of vegetables and the category of fruits. Anything that is an apple is, by definition, a fruit. Apples and vegetables are two separate categories.

### Response:

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There is no overlap between the category of vegetables and the category of fruits. (i.e., If something is a vegetable, it is not a fruit.)
        ∀x (C(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   Premise 2: Anything that is an apple is, by definition, a fruit.
        ∀x (A(x) → B(x))
    *   Conclusion: Apples and vegetables are two separate categories. (i.e., If something is an apple, it is not a vegetable.)
        ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Any animal that is a cat is a mammal. There isn't a single fish that is a cat. This means that no fish is a mammal.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Any animal that is a cat is a mammal. There isn't a single fish that is a cat. This means that no fish is a mammal.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C(x) = x is a cat
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mammal
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fish

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** Any animal that is a cat is a mammal.
        *   ∀x (C(x) → M(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** There isn't a single fish that is a cat.
        *   ¬∃x (F(x) ∧ C(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (F(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** This means that no fish is a mammal.
        *   ¬∃x (F(x) ∧ M(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
This syllogism commits the Fallacy of Illicit Major.
*   The major term is "mammal" (M).
*   In the conclusion "No fish is a mammal" (∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x))), the term "mammal" is distributed (it refers to all mammals being excluded from fish).
*   However, in Premise 1 "All cats are mammals" (∀x (C(x) → M(x))), the term "mammal" is undistributed (it only refers to *some* mammals, specifically those that are cats).
For a syllogism to be valid, any term distributed in the conclusion must also be distributed in at least one of the premises. Since "mammal" is distributed in the conclusion but not in its premise, the argument is invalid.

**Counterexample:**
Consider a scenario where:
*   **Cats (C):** {Siamese}
*   **Mammals (M):** {Siamese, Dog, **Whale**}
*   **Fish (F):** {Salmon, **Whale**}

1.  **Premise 1: All cats are mammals.**
    *   Is {Siamese} ⊆ {Siamese, Dog, Whale}? Yes. (True)
2.  **Premise 2: No fish is a cat.**
    *   Is {Salmon, Whale} ∩ {Siamese} = ∅? Yes. (True)
3.  **Conclusion: No fish is a mammal.**
    *   Is {Salmon, Whale} ∩ {Siamese, Dog, Whale} = ∅? No, the intersection is {Whale}. (False)

Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no polygons that are shapes with three sides. All triangles are shapes with three sides. The implication is that some triangles are not polygons.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no polygons that are shapes with three sides. All triangles are shapes with three sides. The implication is that some triangles are not polygons.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let P = polygons
    Let S = shapes with three sides
    Let T = triangles

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (P(x) → ¬S(x))
    Premise 2: ∀x (T(x) → S(x))
    Conclusion: ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬P(x))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    INVALID

    **Explanation of Validity:**
    Let's assume the premises are true:
    1.  ∀x (P(x) → ¬S(x)) (No polygons are shapes with three sides)
    2.  ∀x (T(x) → S(x)) (All triangles are shapes with three sides)

    From Premise 1, by contraposition, we can infer:
    ∀x (S(x) → ¬P(x)) (If something is a shape with three sides, then it is not a polygon)

    Now, combining Premise 2 and this derived statement using hypothetical syllogism:
    ∀x (T(x) → S(x))
    ∀x (S(x) → ¬P(x))
    Therefore, ∀x (T(x) → ¬P(x)) (No triangles are polygons)

    The conclusion of the syllogism is ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬P(x)) (Some triangles are not polygons).

    In modern predicate logic, a universal statement like "No triangles are polygons" (∀x (T(x) → ¬P(x))) does not necessarily imply an existential statement like "Some triangles are not polygons" (∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬P(x))). The existential conclusion only follows if we assume that the set of "triangles" (T) is not empty.

    Consider a scenario where the set of "triangles" (T) is empty.
    *   If T is empty, then Premise 2 (∀x (T(x) → S(x))) is vacuously true.
    *   If T is empty, then the derived statement "No triangles are polygons" (∀x (T(x) → ¬P(x))) is also vacuously true.
    *   However, if T is empty, the conclusion "Some triangles are not polygons" (∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬P(x))) is false, because there are no triangles to be not polygons.

    Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false (in the case where the set of triangles is empty), the syllogism is **INVALID**.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Nothing that is an animal is a non-living thing. There are some mammals that are not non-living things. It follows that some mammals are not animals.

### Response:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = animals
    Let B = non-living things
    Let C = mammals

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: Nothing that is an animal is a non-living thing. (Equivalent to: All animals are not non-living things.)
    ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))

    Premise 2: There are some mammals that are not non-living things.
    ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬B(x))

    Conclusion: It follows that some mammals are not animals.
    ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every fish can be seen to write novels. Anything that can write novels has feathers. It follows that a few things with feathers are fish.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Every fish can be seen to write novels. Anything that can write novels has feathers. It follows that a few things with feathers are fish.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let F(x) = "x is a fish"
    *   Let N(x) = "x can write novels"
    *   Let H(x) = "x has feathers"

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** Every fish can be seen to write novels.
        *   ∀x (F(x) → N(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** Anything that can write novels has feathers.
        *   ∀x (N(x) → H(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** It follows that a few things with feathers are fish.
        *   ∃x (H(x) ∧ F(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
From Premise 1 and Premise 2, we can logically deduce ∀x (F(x) → H(x)) by hypothetical syllogism (transitivity of implication). This means "Every fish has feathers."

However, in modern symbolic logic, a universal affirmative statement (like "Every fish has feathers") does not imply an existential statement (like "Some fish have feathers" or "A few things with feathers are fish") unless the subject class (fish, in this case) is known to be non-empty.

Consider a scenario where there are no fish (i.e., the set of F is empty).
*   If there are no fish, then F(x) is always false.
*   Premise 1 (∀x (F(x) → N(x))) would be true (vacuously, as false implies anything).
*   Premise 2 (∀x (N(x) → H(x))) could also be true.
*   However, the Conclusion (∃x (H(x) ∧ F(x))) would be false, because there are no x for which F(x) is true.

Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**. This is an example of the existential fallacy.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no fish that are mammals. No cats are considered fish. Some felines are not mammals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no fish that are mammals. No cats are considered fish. Some felines are not mammals.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let F(x) = x is a fish
    Let M(x) = x is a mammal
    Let C(x) = x is a cat
    Let L(x) = x is a feline

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There are no fish that are mammals.
        ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** No cats are considered fish.
        ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Some felines are not mammals.
        ∃x (L(x) ∧ ¬M(x))

    *Implicit Premise (based on common biological understanding): All cats are felines.*
    ∀x (C(x) → L(x))

    To determine validity, we assume the premises (including the implicit one) are true and check if the conclusion *must* logically follow.

    Let's assume the premises are true:
    1.  ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x)) (No fish are mammals)
    2.  ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x)) (No cats are fish)
    3.  ∀x (C(x) → L(x)) (All cats are felines)

    Now, let's assume the conclusion is false. If "Some felines are not mammals" is false, then its negation must be true: "All felines are mammals."
    ¬(∃x (L(x) ∧ ¬M(x))) ≡ ∀x ¬(L(x) ∧ ¬M(x)) ≡ ∀x (¬L(x) ∨ M(x)) ≡ ∀x (L(x) → M(x))
    So, let's assume:
    4.  ∀x (L(x) → M(x)) (All felines are mammals)

    Now we check for consistency.
    From (3) and (4) using Hypothetical Syllogism:
    If x is a cat, then x is a feline (from 3).
    If x is a feline, then x is a mammal (from 4).
    Therefore, if x is a cat, then x is a mammal.
    5.  ∀x (C(x) → M(x)) (All cats are mammals)

    Now we have:
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → ¬M(x)) (No fish are mammals)
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x)) (No cats are fish)
    *   Derived (5): ∀x (C(x) → M(x)) (All cats are mammals)

    These three statements are perfectly consistent. For example, consider a world where:
    *   Fish = {Salmon, Tuna} (none are mammals)
    *   Mammals = {Dogs, Humans, Cats, Lions, Tigers} (none are fish)
    *   Cats = {Siamese, Persian} (are mammals, are not fish)
    *   Felines = {Siamese, Persian, Lion, Tiger} (all are mammals)

    In this scenario:
    *   Premise 1 (No fish are mammals) is true.
    *   Premise 2 (No cats are fish) is true.
    *   Implicit Premise (All cats are felines) is true.
    *   Conclusion (Some felines are not mammals) is **false**, because in this scenario, *all* felines are mammals.

    Since we can construct a scenario where all premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is not true that any ocean is a continent. Every single thing that is an ocean named Pacific is an ocean. This means some oceans named Pacific are not continents.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** It is not true that any ocean is a continent. Every single thing that is an ocean named Pacific is an ocean. This means some oceans named Pacific are not continents.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let O(x) = x is an ocean
    *   Let C(x) = x is a continent
    *   Let P(x) = x is an ocean named Pacific

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "It is not true that any ocean is a continent."
        This is best interpreted as "No ocean is a continent."
        Symbolically: ¬∃x (O(x) ∧ C(x)), which is equivalent to ∀x (O(x) → ¬C(x)).
    *   **Premise 2:** "Every single thing that is an ocean named Pacific is an ocean."
        Symbolically: ∀x (P(x) → O(x)).
    *   **Conclusion:** "This means some oceans named Pacific are not continents."
        Symbolically: ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x)).

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Reasoning:**
        From Premise 1 (∀x (O(x) → ¬C(x))) and Premise 2 (∀x (P(x) → O(x))), we can logically deduce, using hypothetical syllogism, that ∀x (P(x) → ¬C(x)). This means "All oceans named Pacific are not continents."
        However, the conclusion given is "Some oceans named Pacific are not continents" (∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))).
        A universal affirmative statement (or universal negative, as in this case) does not logically entail an existential statement unless the subject class is known to be non-empty. In other words, "All P are not C" does not imply "Some P are not C" if there are no P's at all.
        If the set of "oceans named Pacific" (P) is empty, then Premise 1 and Premise 2 would still be true, and the derived statement "All oceans named Pacific are not continents" would also be true (vacuously). However, the conclusion "Some oceans named Pacific are not continents" would be false.
        Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no vegetables that are not poisonous. Anything that is a carrot is a vegetable. It follows that a number of carrots are poisonous.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no vegetables that are not poisonous. Anything that is a carrot is a vegetable. It follows that a number of carrots are poisonous.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let V(x) = x is a vegetable
    *   Let P(x) = x is poisonous
    *   Let C(x) = x is a carrot

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1: "There are no vegetables that are not poisonous."**
        This means: For all x, if x is a vegetable, then x is poisonous.
        ∀x (V(x) → P(x))
    *   **Premise 2: "Anything that is a carrot is a vegetable."**
        This means: For all x, if x is a carrot, then x is a vegetable.
        ∀x (C(x) → V(x))
    *   **Conclusion: "It follows that a number of carrots are poisonous."**
        "A number of" implies "some" or "at least one".
        This means: There exists at least one x such that x is a carrot and x is poisonous.
        ∃x (C(x) ∧ P(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
From Premise 1 [∀x (V(x) → P(x))] and Premise 2 [∀x (C(x) → V(x))], we can logically deduce, through hypothetical syllogism, that ∀x (C(x) → P(x)), which means "All carrots are poisonous."

However, the conclusion given is ∃x (C(x) ∧ P(x)), which means "Some carrots are poisonous." In standard first-order logic, a universal statement (like "All carrots are poisonous") does not imply an existential statement (like "Some carrots are poisonous") unless it is explicitly stated or assumed that the subject of the universal statement (carrots, in this case) actually exists.

Consider a scenario where there are no carrots in existence. In such a scenario, the statement "All carrots are poisonous" would be vacuously true (because there are no carrots to contradict it). However, the conclusion "Some carrots are poisonous" would be false (because there are no carrots at all). Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
No buildings are found within the group of plants. The category of trees is entirely made up of plants. Buildings and trees are completely separate categories.

ANALYSIS:

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → B(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no roses that are vegetables. A certain quantity of roses are flowers. This logically implies that some flowers are not vegetables.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:** Let R = roses, V = vegetables, F = flowers
2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There are no roses that are vegetables. (Equivalent to: All roses are not vegetables.)
        ∀x (R(x) → ¬V(x))
    *   Premise 2: A certain quantity of roses are flowers. (Equivalent to: Some roses are flowers.)
        ∃x (R(x) ∧ F(x))
    *   Conclusion: Some flowers are not vegetables.
        ∃x (F(x) ∧ ¬V(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is not true that any bird is a cat. No dog is a bird. This leads to the conclusion that no dog is a cat.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** It is not true that any bird is a cat. No dog is a bird. This leads to the conclusion that no dog is a cat.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let B = birds
    *   Let C = cats
    *   Let D = dogs

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: "It is not true that any bird is a cat" means "No bird is a cat."
        *   ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   Premise 2: "No dog is a bird."
        *   ∀x (D(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   Conclusion: "no dog is a cat."
        *   ∀x (D(x) → ¬C(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation for Invalidity:**
        To show invalidity, we need to find a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false.
        Let's assume the conclusion is false, meaning there exists at least one individual 'k' that is both a dog and a cat (∃x (D(x) ∧ C(x))).
        *   If D(k) is true and C(k) is true:
            *   For Premise 1 (∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))) to be true, if C(k) is true, then B(k) must be false (k cannot be a bird).
            *   For Premise 2 (∀x (D(x) → ¬B(x))) to be true, if D(k) is true, then B(k) must be false (k cannot be a bird).
        *   Both premises are consistent with 'k' being a dog and a cat, as long as 'k' is not a bird. This is a perfectly possible scenario. For example, a creature could be a dog and a cat (hypothetically, or in a fictional context where such hybrids exist) without being a bird.
        *   **Counterexample:**
            Consider a universe where:
            *   Dogs (D) = {Fido}
            *   Cats (C) = {Fido}
            *   Birds (B) = {Tweety}
            *   Premise 1: "No bird is a cat." (Tweety is not Fido) - This is true.
            *   Premise 2: "No dog is a bird." (Fido is not Tweety) - This is true.
            *   Conclusion: "No dog is a cat." (Fido is not Fido) - This is false, as Fido is a dog and Fido is a cat.
        Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:

### Response:
ANALYSIS:

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → D(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (A(x) → ¬D(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (A(x) → ¬F(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
All things which are oceans are computers. All things which are oceans are made of silk. It follows that some things made of silk are computers.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let O(x) = x is an ocean (or "x is a thing which is an ocean")
    Let C(x) = x is a computer
    Let S(x) = x is made of silk

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (O(x) → C(x))
    Premise 2: ∀x (O(x) → S(x))
    Conclusion: ∃x (S(x) ∧ C(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    This syllogism is invalid because the premises do not guarantee the existence of any "oceans." In modern predicate logic, universal statements like "All O are C" (∀x (O(x) → C(x))) do not imply that there are any O's. If the set of "things which are oceans" (O) is empty, then both premises are vacuously true (because O(x) is always false, making the implications O(x) → C(x) and O(x) → S(x) always true). However, if there are no oceans, the conclusion "some things made of silk are computers" (∃x (S(x) ∧ C(x))) would be false, as there would be no such things.

    **Counterexample:**
    Consider a world where there are no oceans. Let the domain of discourse be a single object, a "rock."
    - O(rock) = False (a rock is not an ocean)
    - C(rock) = False (a rock is not a computer)
    - S(rock) = False (a rock is not made of silk)

    *   **Premise 1:** ∀x (O(x) → C(x))
        For the "rock": O(rock) → C(rock) is False → False, which is True. So, Premise 1 is True.
    *   **Premise 2:** ∀x (O(x) → S(x))
        For the "rock": O(rock) → S(rock) is False → False, which is True. So, Premise 2 is True.
    *   **Conclusion:** ∃x (S(x) ∧ C(x))
        For the "rock": S(rock) ∧ C(rock) is False ∧ False, which is False. Since there are no other objects, there is no x for which S(x) ∧ C(x) is true. So, the Conclusion is False.

    Since we found a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

    *   Let T = mortals

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no humans who are insects. A few humans are astronauts. It is the case that a portion of all astronauts are insects.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no humans who are insects. A few humans are astronauts. It is the case that a portion of all astronauts are insects.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let H(x) = x is a human
    *   Let I(x) = x is an insect
    *   Let A(x) = x is an astronaut

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There are no humans who are insects. (This means for all x, if x is a human, then x is not an insect.)
        ∀x (H(x) → ¬I(x))
    *   Premise 2: A few humans are astronauts. (This means there exists at least one x such that x is a human and x is an astronaut.)
        ∃x (H(x) ∧ A(x))
    *   Conclusion: It is the case that a portion of all astronauts are insects. (This means there exists at least one x such that x is an astronaut and x is an insect.)
        ∃x (A(x) ∧ I(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
From Premise 2, we know there exists at least one individual, let's call this individual 'k', such that H(k) is true and A(k) is true.
From Premise 1, we know that for any x, if H(x) is true, then ¬I(x) is true. Since H(k) is true, it follows that ¬I(k) is true (i.e., k is not an insect).
So, for this individual 'k', we have:
*   k is a human (H(k))
*   k is an astronaut (A(k))
*   k is not an insect (¬I(k))

The conclusion states that "some astronauts are insects" (∃x (A(x) ∧ I(x))). However, the premises only establish that *at least one* astronaut (namely 'k') is *not* an insect. The premises do not provide any information that forces *any* astronaut to be an insect. It is entirely possible for all astronauts to be non-insects, even if some humans are astronauts and no humans are insects. Therefore, the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises.

### Response:

SYLLOGISM: The group of birds and the group of mammals do not overlap. Anything that is a mammal is an insect. It is the case that some insects are birds.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = birds
    *   Let B = mammals
    *   Let C = insects

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: The group of birds and the group of mammals do not overlap. (No A is B)
        ∀x ¬(A(x) ∧ B(x))
    *   Premise 2: Anything that is a mammal is an insect. (All B are C)
        ∀x (B(x) → C(x))
    *   Conclusion: It is the case that some insects are birds. (Some C are A)
        ∃x (C(x) ∧ A(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single lion is a building. There is no lion which is a skyscraper. Therefore, there are no skyscrapers that are buildings.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let L = lions
    *   Let B = buildings
    *   Let S = skyscrapers

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Every single lion is a building. → ∀x (L(x) → B(x))
    *   Premise 2: There is no lion which is a skyscraper. → ∀x (L(x) → ¬S(x)) (or ¬∃x (L(x) ∧ S(x)))
    *   Conclusion: Therefore, there are no skyscrapers that are buildings. → ∀x (S(x) → ¬B(x)) (or ¬∃x (S(x) ∧ B(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        The premises state that all lions are buildings, and no lions are skyscrapers. This does not logically imply that no skyscrapers are buildings. It is entirely possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false.
        Consider a scenario:
        *   All lions are animals (let's say, a type of building in a fantasy world). (Premise 1 is true)
        *   No lions are skyscrapers (they are small, single-story buildings). (Premise 2 is true)
        *   However, there can still be skyscrapers that *are* buildings (e.g., the Empire State Building is a skyscraper and a building). In this case, the conclusion "there are no skyscrapers that are buildings" would be false.
        Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no humans that are not made of glass. Every single thing made of glass is indestructible. From this, it can be derived that some indestructible things are humans.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no humans that are not made of glass. Every single thing made of glass is indestructible. From this, it can be derived that some indestructible things are humans.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let H = humans
    *   Let G = made of glass
    *   Let I = indestructible things

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: "There are no humans that are not made of glass." This means "All humans are made of glass."
        *   ∀x (H(x) → G(x))
    *   Premise 2: "Every single thing made of glass is indestructible." This means "All things made of glass are indestructible."
        *   ∀x (G(x) → I(x))
    *   Conclusion: "Some indestructible things are humans."
        *   ∃x (I(x) ∧ H(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
From Premise 1 and Premise 2, we can logically derive:
∀x (H(x) → I(x))
This means "All humans are indestructible."

The conclusion is ∃x (I(x) ∧ H(x)), which means "Some indestructible things are humans" (or equivalently, "Some humans are indestructible").

In modern predicate logic, a universal statement like "All humans are indestructible" (∀x (H(x) → I(x))) does not imply the existence of humans. If the set of humans (H) is empty, then the premise "All humans are indestructible" is vacuously true. However, the conclusion "Some humans are indestructible" (∃x (H(x) ∧ I(x))) would be false if there are no humans.

Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false (in the case where the category "humans" is empty), the syllogism is **INVALID**.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no numbers that are tables. It is not true that any table is a couch. It is the case that some couches are numbers.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let N(x) = x is a number
    Let T(x) = x is a table
    Let C(x) = x is a couch

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: There are no numbers that are tables. (Equivalent to: For all x, if x is a number, then x is not a table.)
    ∀x (N(x) → ¬T(x))

    Premise 2: It is not true that any table is a couch. (Equivalent to: For all x, if x is a table, then x is not a couch.)
    ∀x (T(x) → ¬C(x))

    Conclusion: It is the case that some couches are numbers.
    ∃x (C(x) ∧ N(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
No giant balls of gas are stars. Suns are, without exception, giant balls of gas. No star can be considered a sun.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: No giant balls of gas are stars. Suns are, without exception, giant balls of gas. No star can be considered a sun.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let G = giant balls of gas
    *   Let S = stars
    *   Let U = suns

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: No giant balls of gas are stars. (If something is a giant ball of gas, it is not a star.)
        *   ∀x (G(x) → ¬S(x))
    *   Premise 2: Suns are, without exception, giant balls of gas. (If something is a sun, it is a giant ball of gas.)
        *   ∀x (U(x) → G(x))
    *   Conclusion: No star can be considered a sun. (If something is a star, it is not a sun.)
        *   ∀x (S(x) → ¬U(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

    *   **Derivation:**
        1.  From Premise 2: ∀x (U(x) → G(x))
        2.  From Premise 1: ∀x (G(x) → ¬S(x))
        3.  By Hypothetical Syllogism (transitivity of implication) from (1) and (2): ∀x (U(x) → ¬S(x))
            *   This means "No sun is a star."
        4.  The conclusion given is: ∀x (S(x) → ¬U(x))
            *   This means "No star is a sun."
        5.  The statement "No sun is a star" (U(x) → ¬S(x)) is logically equivalent to "No star is a sun" (S(x) → ¬U(x)). Both mean that it's impossible for something to be both a sun and a star (¬∃x (U(x) ∧ S(x))).
    *   Since the conclusion can be logically derived from the premises, the syllogism is VALID.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
No fish are creatures with fur. All of the animals that are considered mammals are creatures with fur. Consequently, it can be said that some mammals are not fish.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** No fish are creatures with fur. All of the animals that are considered mammals are creatures with fur. Consequently, it can be said that some mammals are not fish.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fish
    *   Let U(x) = x is a creature with fur
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mammal

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** No fish are creatures with fur.
        ∀x (F(x) → ¬U(x))
        (Alternatively, ¬∃x (F(x) ∧ U(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** All of the animals that are considered mammals are creatures with fur.
        ∀x (M(x) → U(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Some mammals are not fish.
        ∃x (M(x) ∧ ¬F(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

    *   **Derivation:**
        1.  From Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → ¬U(x)). By contraposition, this is equivalent to ∀x (U(x) → ¬F(x)) (If something has fur, it is not a fish).
        2.  From Premise 2: ∀x (M(x) → U(x)) (If something is a mammal, it has fur).
        3.  Combining the contrapositive of Premise 1 and Premise 2 using hypothetical syllogism:
            ∀x (M(x) → U(x)) ∧ ∀x (U(x) → ¬F(x))  implies  ∀x (M(x) → ¬F(x)).
            This means "No mammals are fish" or "All mammals are not fish."
        4.  In classical syllogistic logic, if a universal negative statement ("No M are F") is true, and the subject term (M, mammals) refers to an existing category (which "mammals" does), then the corresponding particular negative statement ("Some M are not F") must also be true. If no mammals are fish, and mammals exist, then it logically follows that some mammals are not fish.

3. Final Answer: VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no books that are fiction and also non-fiction. Every single fiction book is a story. It follows that some stories fail to be non-fiction.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no books that are fiction and also non-fiction. Every single fiction book is a story. It follows that some stories fail to be non-fiction.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let B(x) = x is a book
    *   Let F(x) = x is fiction
    *   Let N(x) = x is non-fiction
    *   Let S(x) = x is a story

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There are no books that are fiction and also non-fiction.
        *   ∀x ((B(x) ∧ F(x)) → ¬N(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** Every single fiction book is a story.
        *   ∀x ((B(x) ∧ F(x)) → S(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Some stories fail to be non-fiction.
        *   ∃x (S(x) ∧ ¬N(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises are universal statements, while the conclusion is an existential statement. The syllogism is invalid because the premises do not guarantee the existence of any "fiction books" (i.e., any x for which B(x) ∧ F(x) is true). If there are no fiction books, then both premises are vacuously true. However, in such a scenario, the conclusion "some stories fail to be non-fiction" would be false, as there would be no fiction books to serve as examples of stories that are not non-fiction. For the conclusion to follow, the existence of at least one fiction book would need to be asserted or implied by the premises, which it is not.

    *   Conclusion: There are no wolves which are mammals.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no cars which are airplanes. Some of the things that are vehicles are cars. It must be true that some airplanes are not vehicles.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no cars which are airplanes. Some of the things that are vehicles are cars. It must be true that some airplanes are not vehicles.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C = cars
    *   Let A = airplanes
    *   Let V = vehicles

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There are no cars which are airplanes."
        *   ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))
        *   (Alternatively: ¬∃x (C(x) ∧ A(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Some of the things that are vehicles are cars."
        *   ∃x (V(x) ∧ C(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "It must be true that some airplanes are not vehicles."
        *   ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬V(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation for Invalidity:**
        Consider a scenario where:
        *   The set of **Cars (C)** = {car1}
        *   The set of **Vehicles (V)** = {car1, truck1}
        *   The set of **Airplanes (A)** = {} (empty set)

        Let's check the premises and conclusion:
        *   **Premise 1 (True):** "There are no cars which are airplanes."
            Since the set of airplanes is empty, there are no cars that are also airplanes. This premise holds.
        *   **Premise 2 (True):** "Some of the things that are vehicles are cars."
            'car1' is both a vehicle and a car. This premise holds.
        *   **Conclusion (False):** "It must be true that some airplanes are not vehicles."
            Since the set of airplanes is empty, there are no airplanes at all. Therefore, it cannot be true that some airplanes are not vehicles. The conclusion is false.

        Since we found a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Nothing that is a chair is a piece of furniture. A thing that is a table is never a chair. It follows that a table is never a piece of furniture.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Nothing that is a chair is a piece of furniture. A thing that is a table is never a chair. It follows that a table is never a piece of furniture.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = things that are chairs
    Let B = things that are pieces of furniture
    Let C = things that are tables

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))  (Nothing that is a chair is a piece of furniture)
    Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))  (A thing that is a table is never a chair)
    Conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → ¬B(x)) (A table is never a piece of furniture)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        This syllogism is invalid because the premises do not logically necessitate the conclusion.
        Let's assume the premises are true:
        1.  Chairs and furniture are entirely separate categories (no chair is furniture).
        2.  Tables and chairs are entirely separate categories (no table is a chair).

        From these two premises, we cannot conclude that tables and furniture are entirely separate categories. It is possible for something to be a table and a piece of furniture, while still satisfying both premises.

        Consider a counterexample:
        Let A = {chairs}
        Let B = {sofas, beds, *tables*} (i.e., tables are furniture)
        Let C = {tables}

        *   **Premise 1 check:** "Nothing that is a chair is a piece of furniture."
            If chairs are not furniture, this premise holds. (e.g., A and B are disjoint).
        *   **Premise 2 check:** "A thing that is a table is never a chair."
            Tables are not chairs, so this premise holds. (C and A are disjoint).
        *   **Conclusion check:** "A table is never a piece of furniture."
            In our counterexample, tables *are* pieces of furniture (C is a subset of B). Therefore, the conclusion is false.

        Since we can construct a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid. The premises establish that A is disjoint from B, and C is disjoint from A, but they do not establish a necessary disjointness between C and B. C could still overlap with B, as long as it doesn't overlap with A.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything that is a triangle is not a shape with three sides. Every single thing that is an equilateral triangle is a triangle. Therefore, it is the case that some equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Anything that is a triangle is not a shape with three sides. Every single thing that is an equilateral triangle is a triangle. Therefore, it is the case that some equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let T(x) = x is a triangle
    *   Let S(x) = x is a shape with three sides
    *   Let E(x) = x is an equilateral triangle

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (T(x) → ¬S(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (E(x) → T(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (E(x) ∧ ¬S(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

    *   **Explanation of Validity:**
        1.  From Premise 2 (∀x (E(x) → T(x))) and Premise 1 (∀x (T(x) → ¬S(x))), we can infer a new universal statement using the principle of hypothetical syllogism (transitivity of implication): ∀x (E(x) → ¬S(x)). This means "All equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides."
        2.  The conclusion is "Some equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides" (∃x (E(x) ∧ ¬S(x))).
        3.  In traditional syllogistic logic, if a universal affirmative or negative statement is true (e.g., "All E are not S"), and the subject term (E, equilateral triangles) is assumed to refer to existing entities (which is a common assumption for concrete categories in syllogisms), then the corresponding particular statement ("Some E are not S") is also true. Since equilateral triangles are known to exist, the inference from "All equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides" to "Some equilateral triangles are not shapes with three sides" is valid.

SYLLOGISM: Nothing that is a canine is a cat. A certain number of dogs are canines. Thus, some dogs are cats.

    *   Let C(x) = x is a canine
    *   Let T(x) = x is a cat
    *   Let D(x) = x is a dog

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (C(x) → ¬T(x)) (or equivalently, ¬∃x (C(x) ∧ T(x)))
    *   Premise 2: ∃x (D(x) ∧ C(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (D(x) ∧ T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

        ∀x (C(x) → A(x))

        ∀x (C(x) → B(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Lions are not gazelles. All African lions are things that are lions. It follows that there are some African lions that are not gazelles.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Lions are not gazelles. All African lions are things that are lions. It follows that there are some African lions that are not gazelles.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let L(x) = x is a lion
    *   Let G(x) = x is a gazelle
    *   Let A(x) = x is an African lion

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "Lions are not gazelles." (No lion is a gazelle)
        ∀x (L(x) → ¬G(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "All African lions are things that are lions." (All African lions are lions)
        ∀x (A(x) → L(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "It follows that there are some African lions that are not gazelles."
        ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬G(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation:** From Premise 1 and Premise 2, we can logically deduce that "All African lions are not gazelles" (i.e., ∀x (A(x) → ¬G(x))) using the hypothetical syllogism rule.
    *   However, the conclusion given is "There are some African lions that are not gazelles" (i.e., ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬G(x))).
    *   In modern predicate logic, a universal statement (like "All African lions are not gazelles") does not imply an existential statement (like "Some African lions are not gazelles") unless the subject class (African lions) is known to be non-empty.
    *   Consider a scenario where there are no African lions. In this case, both premises would be true (vacuously for Premise 2, and potentially true for Premise 1). However, the conclusion "There are some African lions that are not gazelles" would be false, as there are no African lions at all. Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is the case that no mammal is an animal. There are some dogs that are animals. It must be the case that some dogs are not mammals.

### Response:

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (M(x) → ¬A(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∃x (D(x) ∧ A(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬M(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
The group of humans and the group of people have nothing in common. All of the things that are men are people. Men are not humans.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let H(x) = "x is a human"
    *   Let P(x) = "x is a person"
    *   Let M(x) = "x is a man"

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1: "The group of humans and the group of people have nothing in common."**
        This means there is no individual that is both a human and a person.
        ∀x ¬(H(x) ∧ P(x))
        This is logically equivalent to: ∀x (H(x) → ¬P(x)) or ∀x (P(x) → ¬H(x)). We will use ∀x (P(x) → ¬H(x)) for easier chaining.
    *   **Premise 2: "All of the things that are men are people."**
        ∀x (M(x) → P(x))
    *   **Conclusion: "Men are not humans."**
        ∀x (M(x) → ¬H(x))

    Therefore, the logical formulation is:
    Premise 1: ∀x (P(x) → ¬H(x))
    Premise 2: ∀x (M(x) → P(x))
    Conclusion: ∀x (M(x) → ¬H(x))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    This syllogism follows the form of a hypothetical syllogism (chain argument):
    If M(x) then P(x) (Premise 2)
    If P(x) then ¬H(x) (Premise 1)
    Therefore, if M(x) then ¬H(x) (Conclusion)

    The conclusion necessarily follows from the premises.
    VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Stars are in no way celestial bodies. Anything that is a star is a planet. It's the case that some planets are not celestial bodies.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Stars are in no way celestial bodies. Anything that is a star is a planet. It's the case that some planets are not celestial bodies.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let S(x) = x is a star
    *   Let C(x) = x is a celestial body
    *   Let P(x) = x is a planet

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "Stars are in no way celestial bodies." (No stars are celestial bodies.)
        ∀x (S(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Anything that is a star is a planet." (All stars are planets.)
        ∀x (S(x) → P(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "It's the case that some planets are not celestial bodies." (Some planets are not celestial bodies.)
        ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
In modern symbolic logic, universal statements (like "All S are P") do not carry existential import, meaning they do not guarantee that S actually exists. The premises state properties about stars. If there are no stars (i.e., the set S is empty), both premises become vacuously true.

Consider a scenario where the set of stars (S) is empty:
*   **Premise 1:** ∀x (S(x) → ¬C(x)) is true (because S(x) is always false, making the implication true).
*   **Premise 2:** ∀x (S(x) → P(x)) is true (because S(x) is always false, making the implication true).

Now, let's see if the conclusion must be true in this scenario.
*   **Conclusion:** ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))

If S is empty, the premises are true. However, the conclusion is an existential statement, requiring at least one entity that is a planet and not a celestial body. The premises, being vacuously true, do not guarantee the existence of such an entity.

**Counterexample:**
Let the domain of discourse be a single object, 'a'.
Let S(a) be False (a is not a star).
Let P(a) be True (a is a planet).
Let C(a) be True (a is a celestial body).

*   **Premise 1:** S(a) → ¬C(a) becomes False → False, which is True.
*   **Premise 2:** S(a) → P(a) becomes False → True, which is True.
*   **Conclusion:** P(a) ∧ ¬C(a) becomes True ∧ False, which is False.

Since we found a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is no overlap between even numbers and words. All words are numbers that can be divided by two. Therefore, some numbers divisible by two are not even.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There is no overlap between even numbers and words. All words are numbers that can be divided by two. Therefore, some numbers divisible by two are not even.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A(x) = x is an even number
    *   Let B(x) = x is a word
    *   Let C(x) = x is a number divisible by two

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There is no overlap between even numbers and words.
        ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
        (Alternatively: ¬∃x (A(x) ∧ B(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** All words are numbers that can be divided by two.
        ∀x (B(x) → C(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Some numbers divisible by two are not even.
        ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    **INVALID**

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    The syllogism is invalid because the premises do not guarantee the existence of any "words" (B(x)). If there are no words, both premises become vacuously true.

    *   If there are no words (i.e., ∀x ¬B(x)), then:
        *   Premise 1 (∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))) is true because ¬B(x) is always true.
        *   Premise 2 (∀x (B(x) → C(x))) is true because B(x) is always false, making the implication true.

    *   Now, consider the conclusion (∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))) under the assumption that there are no words. The conclusion states that there exists something that is a number divisible by two but is not an even number.
        In standard mathematics, "even numbers" and "numbers divisible by two" refer to the exact same set of numbers. If we assume A(x) and C(x) are equivalent (i.e., ∀x (A(x) ↔ C(x))), then the conclusion becomes ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬A(x)), which is a contradiction and therefore false.

    *   **Counterexample:**
        Let the universe of discourse be integers.
        *   Let A = {2, 4, 6} (even numbers)
        *   Let B = {} (words - an empty set)
        *   Let C = {2, 4, 6} (numbers divisible by two - identical to A)

        *   **Premise 1:** There is no overlap between A and B. (True, since B is empty).
        *   **Premise 2:** All B are C. (True, since B is empty, the statement is vacuously true).
        *   **Conclusion:** Some C are not A. (False, because C and A are identical sets, so there are no elements in C that are not in A).

    Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single dog is a mammal. A portion of mammals can be classified as creatures that bark. Some creatures that can bark are not dogs.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Every single dog is a mammal. A portion of mammals can be classified as creatures that bark. Some creatures that can bark are not dogs.

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let D = dogs
    *   Let M = mammals
    *   Let B = creatures that bark

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (D(x) → M(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∃x (M(x) ∧ B(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬D(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises. Consider a scenario where all creatures that bark are also dogs.

*   Let D = Poodles
*   Let M = Dogs
*   Let B = Poodles (i.e., assume that in this specific context, the "creatures that bark" referred to are exclusively poodles)

1.  **Premise 1:** Every single Poodle is a Dog. (True)
2.  **Premise 2:** A portion of Dogs can be classified as Poodles. (True, since Poodles are Dogs, some Dogs are Poodles)
3.  **Conclusion:** Some Poodles are not Poodles. (False, as no Poodle is not a Poodle)

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Input:
Every single human is a monkey. There are no primates that are monkeys. From this, it follows that no primate is a human.

### Response:

    *   Let H = humans
    *   Let M = monkeys
    *   Let P = primates

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Every single human is a monkey. → ∀x (H(x) → M(x))
    *   Premise 2: There are no primates that are monkeys. → ∀x (P(x) → ¬M(x))
    *   Conclusion: No primate is a human. → ∀x (P(x) → ¬H(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is no overlap between living things that can breathe oxygen and rocks. All things that are rocks are animals. Certain animals are not living things that can breathe oxygen.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There is no overlap between living things that can breathe oxygen and rocks. All things that are rocks are animals. Certain animals are not living things that can breathe oxygen.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let O(x) = x is a living thing that can breathe oxygen
    *   Let R(x) = x is a rock
    *   Let A(x) = x is an animal

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There is no overlap between living things that can breathe oxygen and rocks.
        *   ∀x (O(x) → ¬R(x))  (or equivalently, ¬∃x (O(x) ∧ R(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** All things that are rocks are animals.
        *   ∀x (R(x) → A(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Certain animals are not living things that can breathe oxygen.
        *   ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation:**
        From Premise 1, we know that if something breathes oxygen, it's not a rock. The contrapositive of this is: if something is a rock, it does not breathe oxygen (∀x (R(x) → ¬O(x))).
        From Premise 2, we know that all rocks are animals (∀x (R(x) → A(x))).
        Combining these two derived statements about rocks, we can infer that all rocks are animals that do not breathe oxygen: ∀x (R(x) → (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x))).

        The conclusion is an existential statement: "Some animals are not living things that can breathe oxygen" (∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x))). This conclusion would be true if there exists at least one rock, because any rock would satisfy the condition of being an animal that does not breathe oxygen.

        However, the premises are universal statements and do not guarantee the existence of rocks. It is possible for the premises to be true even if there are no rocks.
        Consider a scenario where there are no rocks in the universe.
        *   **Premise 1:** "There is no overlap between living things that can breathe oxygen and rocks." (True, vacuously, since there are no rocks to overlap with).
        *   **Premise 2:** "All things that are rocks are animals." (True, vacuously, since there are no rocks).
        *   **Conclusion:** "Certain animals are not living things that can breathe oxygen." (This could be false. For example, if all existing animals *do* breathe oxygen, then the conclusion is false).

        Since we can construct a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**. This is an example of the existential fallacy, where an existential conclusion is drawn from purely universal premises without an explicit assertion of existence.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything that is a technology is a piece of furniture. No furniture is a phone. It is therefore a sound deduction that some phones is not technology.

### Response:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let T = technology
    *   Let F = furniture
    *   Let P = phone

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (T(x) → F(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (F(x) → ¬P(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    *   From Premise 1 and Premise 2, by the rule of Hypothetical Syllogism, we can deduce: ∀x (T(x) → ¬P(x)) (i.e., "All technology is not a phone").
    *   By contraposition, this is equivalent to: ∀x (P(x) → ¬T(x)) (i.e., "All phones are not technology").
    *   The conclusion given is ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬T(x)) (i.e., "Some phones are not technology").
    *   In modern predicate logic, a universal statement (like "All P are not T") does not imply the existence of its subject (P). If the set of phones (P) is empty, then "All phones are not technology" is vacuously true, but "Some phones are not technology" would be false. Therefore, the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises.

    INVALID

    *   Let V(x) = x is a vertebrate

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
The category of moons and the category of celestial bodies do not overlap. All celestial bodies are planets. A subset of planets are moons.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let M = moons
    *   Let C = celestial bodies
    *   Let P = planets

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: The category of moons and the category of celestial bodies do not overlap.
        *   ∀x (M(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   Premise 2: All celestial bodies are planets.
        *   ∀x (C(x) → P(x))
    *   Conclusion: A subset of planets are moons. (Interpreted as "Some planets are moons")
        *   ∃x (P(x) ∧ M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        The premises state that moons and celestial bodies are distinct categories, and all celestial bodies are planets. These premises do not guarantee that any planets are moons.
        Consider a scenario where:
        *   M = {Luna} (a moon)
        *   C = {Mars, Jupiter} (celestial bodies)
        *   P = {Mars, Jupiter, Saturn} (planets)

        Let's check the premises:
        1.  "Moons and celestial bodies do not overlap": Luna is not in {Mars, Jupiter}. (True)
        2.  "All celestial bodies are planets": {Mars, Jupiter} is a subset of {Mars, Jupiter, Saturn}. (True)

        Now, let's check the conclusion:
        "A subset of planets are moons" (i.e., Some planets are moons): Is there any element common to P and M? {Mars, Jupiter, Saturn} ∩ {Luna} = ∅. (False)

        Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no eagles that are mammals. Every single eagle is a bird. It is implied that some birds are not mammals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no eagles that are mammals. Every single eagle is a bird. It is implied that some birds are not mammals.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let E = eagles
    *   Let M = mammals
    *   Let B = birds

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There are no eagles that are mammals."
        *   ∀x (E(x) → ¬M(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Every single eagle is a bird."
        *   ∀x (E(x) → B(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "It is implied that some birds are not mammals."
        *   ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation for Invalidity:**
In modern predicate logic, universal statements (like "All A are B" or "No A are B") do not imply the existence of the subject (A). The premises state relationships between eagles, mammals, and birds, but they do not guarantee that any eagles actually exist.

If we assume there are no eagles (i.e., the set of eagles is empty), then:
*   **Premise 1** (∀x (E(x) → ¬M(x))) becomes vacuously true, as the antecedent E(x) is always false.
*   **Premise 2** (∀x (E(x) → B(x))) also becomes vacuously true for the same reason.

However, the **Conclusion** (∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬M(x))) asserts the existence of at least one bird that is not a mammal. If there are no eagles, the premises are true, but the conclusion might be false. For example, consider a world where there are no eagles, and all existing birds are also mammals (e.g., a world with only bats and no other birds). In such a scenario, the premises would be true, but the conclusion would be false, demonstrating the invalidity of the argument. The conclusion only follows if the existence of eagles is assumed.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything which is a bird is an animal. No birds are rocks. Therefore, It is possible to infer that some rocks are animals.

### Response:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let B = birds
    *   Let A = animals
    *   Let R = rocks

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (B(x) → A(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → ¬R(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (R(x) ∧ A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID
