### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no trees that cannot teleport. Every single thing that can teleport has gills. It is a logical consequence that some things with gills are trees.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no trees that cannot teleport. Every single thing that can teleport has gills. It is a logical consequence that some things with gills are trees.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let T(x) = x is a tree
    *   Let P(x) = x can teleport
    *   Let G(x) = x has gills

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1: "There are no trees that cannot teleport."**
        This means: For all x, if x is a tree, then x can teleport.
        ∀x (T(x) → P(x))
    *   **Premise 2: "Every single thing that can teleport has gills."**
        This means: For all x, if x can teleport, then x has gills.
        ∀x (P(x) → G(x))
    *   **Conclusion: "It is a logical consequence that some things with gills are trees."**
        This means: There exists at least one x such that x has gills and x is a tree.
        ∃x (G(x) ∧ T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation:**
    From Premise 1 (∀x (T(x) → P(x))) and Premise 2 (∀x (P(x) → G(x))), we can logically infer, through hypothetical syllogism, that:
    ∀x (T(x) → G(x))
    This means "All trees have gills."

    However, the conclusion is "Some things with gills are trees" (∃x (G(x) ∧ T(x))), which is equivalent to "Some trees have gills."

    In modern symbolic logic, a universal statement (like "All trees have gills") does not necessarily imply an existential statement (like "Some trees have gills"). If there are no trees in the domain of discourse, then "All trees have gills" is vacuously true (because the antecedent T(x) is always false), but "Some trees have gills" would be false.

    Consider a scenario where the domain of discourse is non-empty, but there are no trees. In such a scenario:
    *   Premise 1 (∀x (T(x) → P(x))) would be true.
    *   Premise 2 (∀x (P(x) → G(x))) could also be true (e.g., if there are no things that can teleport, or if all things that can teleport also have gills).
    *   But the Conclusion (∃x (G(x) ∧ T(x))) would be false, because there are no trees (and thus no trees with gills).

    Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything that is a dog has fur. There are some poodles that are dogs. There are no poodles that do not have fur.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Anything that is a dog has fur. There are some poodles that are dogs. There are no poodles that do not have fur.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let D(x) = x is a dog
    *   Let F(x) = x has fur
    *   Let P(x) = x is a poodle

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** Anything that is a dog has fur.
        *   ∀x (D(x) → F(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** There are some poodles that are dogs.
        *   ∃x (P(x) ∧ D(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** There are no poodles that do not have fur. (This is equivalent to "All poodles have fur.")
        *   ∀x (P(x) → F(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Some aquatic animals can be considered fish. Not a single human is an aquatic animal. As a result, no human is a fish.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Some aquatic animals can be considered fish. Not a single human is an aquatic animal. As a result, no human is a fish.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = aquatic animals
    *   Let B = fish
    *   Let C = humans

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∃x (A(x) ∧ B(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → ¬B(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The syllogism is invalid because the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises.
*   Premise 1 states that there is at least one thing that is both an aquatic animal and a fish.
*   Premise 2 states that no human is an aquatic animal.
*   The conclusion states that no human is a fish.

Consider a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false:
*   Let A = {cats, dogs} (representing aquatic animals)
*   Let B = {cats, birds} (representing fish)
*   Let C = {birds, humans} (representing humans)

1.  **Premise 1: ∃x (A(x) ∧ B(x))**
    *   Is there something that is both in A and B? Yes, 'cats' are in both sets. (TRUE)

2.  **Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))**
    *   Is it true that if something is in C, it is not in A?
        *   If x is a 'bird' (in C), it is not in A ({cats, dogs}). (TRUE)
        *   If x is a 'human' (in C), it is not in A ({cats, dogs}). (TRUE)
    *   So, Premise 2 is true.

3.  **Conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → ¬B(x))**
    *   Is it true that if something is in C, it is not in B?
        *   If x is a 'bird' (in C), is it not in B ({cats, birds})? No, 'birds' *are* in B.
    *   Therefore, the statement "If x is a bird, then x is not in B" is false. This makes the entire conclusion false.

Since we found a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid. The existence of "fish" that are not "aquatic animals" (which is not ruled out by Premise 1) could potentially be "humans" without violating Premise 2.

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Not a single one of the things that are penguins is a flying bird. It is known that some birds are flying birds. The conclusion is that some birds are not penguins.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Not a single one of the things that are penguins is a flying bird. It is known that some birds are flying birds. The conclusion is that some birds are not penguins.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let P(x) = x is a penguin
    *   Let F(x) = x is a flying bird
    *   Let B(x) = x is a bird

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** Not a single one of the things that are penguins is a flying bird.
        *   ∀x (P(x) → ¬F(x))  (If something is a penguin, it is not a flying bird)
        *   (Alternatively: ¬∃x (P(x) ∧ F(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** It is known that some birds are flying birds.
        *   ∃x (B(x) ∧ F(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** The conclusion is that some birds are not penguins.
        *   ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬P(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Fathers are in no way humans. Anything that is a father is a mother. There are some mothers that are not humans.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Fathers are in no way humans. Anything that is a father is a mother. There are some mothers that are not humans.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let F = fathers
    *   Let H = humans
    *   Let M = mothers

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Fathers are in no way humans. (No F are H)
        *   ∀x (F(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   Premise 2: Anything that is a father is a mother. (All F are M)
        *   ∀x (F(x) → M(x))
    *   Conclusion: There are some mothers that are not humans. (Some M are not H)
        *   ∃x (M(x) ∧ ¬H(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The syllogism is invalid because the conclusion, which asserts the existence of something (some mothers that are not humans), cannot be necessarily derived from two universal premises without an additional premise asserting existential import.

Consider a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false:
*   Assume there are no fathers (i.e., the set F is empty).
    *   Premise 1 (∀x (F(x) → ¬H(x))) is true vacuously, as F(x) is always false.
    *   Premise 2 (∀x (F(x) → M(x))) is true vacuously, as F(x) is always false.
*   Now, let's assume the conclusion is false. If ∃x (M(x) ∧ ¬H(x)) is false, it means that there are no mothers that are not humans, or equivalently, all mothers are humans (∀x (M(x) → H(x))).
*   In a world with no fathers, and where all mothers are humans, both premises are true, but the conclusion is false. For example, if the only individuals are human mothers, and there are no fathers, then:
    *   P1: True (no fathers, so no fathers are humans).
    *   P2: True (no fathers, so all fathers are mothers).
    *   Conclusion: False (all mothers are humans, so there are no mothers that are not humans).

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

        ∀x (D(x) → ¬C(x))

        ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is no worm that is an insect. Every worm is a bird. This leads to the conclusion that all birds are insects.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There is no worm that is an insect. Every worm is a bird. This leads to the conclusion that all birds are insects.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let W(x) = x is a worm
    Let I(x) = x is an insect
    Let B(x) = x is a bird

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: There is no worm that is an insect. (This means for all x, if x is a worm, then x is not an insect.)
    ∀x (W(x) → ¬I(x))

    Premise 2: Every worm is a bird. (This means for all x, if x is a worm, then x is a bird.)
    ∀x (W(x) → B(x))

    Conclusion: All birds are insects. (This means for all x, if x is a bird, then x is an insect.)
    ∀x (B(x) → I(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    To show this is invalid, we need to demonstrate a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false.

    *   **Premise 1:** ∀x (W(x) → ¬I(x)) - No worm is an insect.
    *   **Premise 2:** ∀x (W(x) → B(x)) - All worms are birds.

    From these premises, if there exists at least one worm (let's call it 'Wormy'), then:
    1.  Wormy is not an insect (from Premise 1).
    2.  Wormy is a bird (from Premise 2).

    So, we have an entity (Wormy) that is a bird but is not an insect.
    Now, let's look at the conclusion: ∀x (B(x) → I(x)) - All birds are insects.
    If Wormy is a bird (B(Wormy) is true) and Wormy is not an insect (I(Wormy) is false), then the statement B(Wormy) → I(Wormy) is false.
    Since we found a bird (Wormy) that is not an insect, the conclusion "All birds are insects" is false.

    Therefore, the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, making the syllogism invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single animal that is a spider is a creature with eight legs. A few of the things known as daddy longlegs are creatures with eight legs. As a result, not all daddy longlegs are spiders.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Every single animal that is a spider is a creature with eight legs. A few of the things known as daddy longlegs are creatures with eight legs. As a result, not all daddy longlegs are spiders.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let S(x) = x is a spider
    *   Let E(x) = x is a creature with eight legs
    *   Let D(x) = x is a daddy longlegs

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** ∀x (S(x) → E(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** ∃x (D(x) ∧ E(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** ∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬S(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
To show the syllogism is invalid, we need to find a scenario (a model) where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false.

If the conclusion "∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬S(x))" (Some daddy longlegs are not spiders) is false, then its negation must be true. The negation is "¬∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬S(x))", which is equivalent to "∀x ¬(D(x) ∧ ¬S(x))", which simplifies to "∀x (¬D(x) ∨ S(x))", and finally to "∀x (D(x) → S(x))" (All daddy longlegs are spiders).

Let's construct a model where:
*   Premise 1: ∀x (S(x) → E(x)) is true.
*   Premise 2: ∃x (D(x) ∧ E(x)) is true.
*   Negation of Conclusion: ∀x (D(x) → S(x)) is true.

Consider a world where:
*   All spiders are eight-legged creatures. (S ⊆ E)
*   All daddy longlegs are spiders. (D ⊆ S)

From these two conditions, it logically follows that all daddy longlegs are eight-legged creatures (D ⊆ E).

Now, let's check the premises and the negation of the conclusion:
*   **Premise 1 (∀x (S(x) → E(x))):** This is true by our setup (S ⊆ E).
*   **Premise 2 (∃x (D(x) ∧ E(x))):** If there is at least one daddy longlegs (which is usually assumed for existential statements to be meaningful), then since all daddy longlegs are eight-legged (D ⊆ E), it must be true that some daddy longlegs are eight-legged. So, this premise is true.
*   **Negation of Conclusion (∀x (D(x) → S(x))):** This is true by our setup (D ⊆ S).

Since we can create a consistent scenario where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false (because its negation is true), the syllogism is **INVALID**. The premises do not guarantee that some daddy longlegs are not spiders; it's possible that all daddy longlegs *are* spiders (and thus also eight-legged).

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is not true that any insect is a creature with eight legs. Every insect is a spider. It follows that every spider is a creature with eight legs.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: It is not true that any insect is a creature with eight legs. Every insect is a spider. It follows that every spider is a creature with eight legs.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A(x) = x is an insect
    Let B(x) = x is a creature with eight legs
    Let C(x) = x is a spider

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1: "It is not true that any insect is a creature with eight legs."**
        This statement is best interpreted as "No insect is a creature with eight legs."
        Symbolically: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   **Premise 2: "Every insect is a spider."**
        Symbolically: ∀x (A(x) → C(x))
    *   **Conclusion: "It follows that every spider is a creature with eight legs."**
        Symbolically: ∀x (C(x) → B(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Validity Check:**
    To check validity, we assume the premises are true and see if the conclusion *must* be true.

    Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x)) (All insects do not have eight legs)
    Premise 2: ∀x (A(x) → C(x)) (All insects are spiders)

    Conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → B(x)) (All spiders have eight legs)

    Let's try to find a counterexample where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false.
    Assume there exists at least one insect, let's call it 'k'. So, A(k) is true.

    From Premise 1: Since A(k) is true, it must be that ¬B(k) is true (k does not have eight legs).
    From Premise 2: Since A(k) is true, it must be that C(k) is true (k is a spider).

    So, for this insect 'k', we know:
    *   C(k) is true (k is a spider)
    *   B(k) is false (k does not have eight legs)

    Now, let's look at the conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → B(x)).
    For our specific 'k', the statement C(k) → B(k) becomes (True → False), which is False.
    Since we found an instance ('k') for which the conclusion's predicate (C(x) → B(x)) is false, the universal conclusion (∀x (C(x) → B(x))) is false.

    Therefore, the conclusion does not logically follow from the premises. The syllogism is **INVALID**.

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

2. Logical Formulation: Premise 1: ∀x (D(x) → C(x)). Premise 2: ∀x (K(x) → C(x)). Conclusion: ∀x (K(x) → D(x))
3. Final Answer: INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There exist no machines that are also humans. Anything that can be called a student is, without exception, a human. No students are machines.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:** Let M = machines, H = humans, S = students
2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There exist no machines that are also humans.
        ¬∃x (M(x) ∧ H(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (M(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   Premise 2: Anything that can be called a student is, without exception, a human.
        ∀x (S(x) → H(x))
    *   Conclusion: No students are machines.
        ¬∃x (S(x) ∧ M(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (S(x) → ¬M(x))

    Therefore:
    Premise 1: ∀x (M(x) → ¬H(x))
    Premise 2: ∀x (S(x) → H(x))
    Conclusion: ∀x (S(x) → ¬M(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Mountains cannot be oceans. Every mountain named Everest is a mountain. It must be that some mountains named Everest are not oceans.

### Response:
**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mountain
    *   Let O(x) = x is an ocean
    *   Let A(x) = x is a mountain named Everest

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (M(x) → ¬O(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (A(x) → M(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    From Premise 1 and Premise 2, by the rule of Hypothetical Syllogism, we can derive:
    ∀x (A(x) → ¬O(x))
    This derived statement means "All mountains named Everest are not oceans."

    The conclusion of the syllogism is ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x)), which means "Some mountains named Everest are not oceans."

    In modern predicate logic, a universal statement (like "All A are not O") does not imply the existence of its subject. If the set of "mountains named Everest" (A) is empty, then the derived statement ∀x (A(x) → ¬O(x)) is vacuously true. However, the conclusion ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬O(x)) would be false if A is empty. Since it is possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false (if there are no mountains named Everest), the syllogism is **INVALID**.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is nothing that is a vegetable that is also a fruit. Every single thing that is a carrot is a vegetable. As a result, at least one thing that is a carrot is not a fruit.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let V(x) = x is a vegetable
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fruit
    *   Let C(x) = x is a carrot

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There is nothing that is a vegetable that is also a fruit.
        *   ¬∃x (V(x) ∧ F(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (V(x) → ¬F(x))
    *   Premise 2: Every single thing that is a carrot is a vegetable.
        *   ∀x (C(x) → V(x))
    *   Conclusion: As a result, at least one thing that is a carrot is not a fruit.
        *   ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬F(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
From Premise 1 (∀x (V(x) → ¬F(x))) and Premise 2 (∀x (C(x) → V(x))), we can logically deduce ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x)) (i.e., "All carrots are not fruits").

However, in modern symbolic logic, a universal statement like ∀x (C(x) → ¬F(x)) does *not* automatically imply an existential statement like ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬F(x)). The universal statement would be true even if there were no carrots at all (it would be vacuously true). But if there are no carrots, then the existential conclusion ("at least one thing that is a carrot is not a fruit") would be false.

For example, consider a hypothetical world where carrots do not exist.
*   Premise 1: "There is nothing that is a vegetable that is also a fruit" could still be true (e.g., apples are fruits, potatoes are vegetables, no overlap).
*   Premise 2: "Every single thing that is a carrot is a vegetable" would be vacuously true because there are no carrots to contradict it.
*   Conclusion: "At least one thing that is a carrot is not a fruit" would be false because there are no carrots at all.

Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no parrots which are mammals. Some birds are parrots. This implies that some birds are mammals.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let P = parrots
    *   Let M = mammals
    *   Let B = birds

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: There are no parrots which are mammals. (Equivalently: All parrots are not mammals.)
        *   ∀x (P(x) → ¬M(x))
    *   Premise 2: Some birds are parrots.
        *   ∃x (B(x) ∧ P(x))
    *   Conclusion: Some birds are mammals.
        *   ∃x (B(x) ∧ M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
From Premise 2, we know there exists at least one individual, let's call it 'k', such that 'k' is a bird (B(k)) and 'k' is a parrot (P(k)).
From Premise 1, we know that if something is a parrot, it is not a mammal (P(x) → ¬M(x)). Since 'k' is a parrot (P(k)), it follows that 'k' is not a mammal (¬M(k)).
So, for this individual 'k', we have B(k) and ¬M(k). This means that the 'some birds' that are parrots are *not* mammals. The premises provide no information about any other birds that *might* be mammals. Therefore, the conclusion that "some birds are mammals" does not logically follow from the given premises.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Birds are in no way winged creatures. There exist no winged creatures that are animals. It is the case that no bird is an animal.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Birds are in no way winged creatures. There exist no winged creatures that are animals. It is the case that no bird is an animal.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = birds
    *   Let B = winged creatures
    *   Let C = animals

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Birds are in no way winged creatures. (No A are B)
        ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x))
    *   Premise 2: There exist no winged creatures that are animals. (No B are C)
        ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   Conclusion: It is the case that no bird is an animal. (No A are C)
        ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises state that A and B are disjoint, and B and C are disjoint. This does not logically imply that A and C must be disjoint. Both A and C could exist entirely outside of B, and still overlap with each other.

Consider a counterexample:
*   Let A = "dogs"
*   Let B = "cats"
*   Let C = "mammals"

*   Premise 1: No dogs are cats. (True: ∀x (Dog(x) → ¬Cat(x)))
*   Premise 2: No cats are mammals. (False in reality, but let's assume it's true for the sake of the logical structure, e.g., if B were "reptiles" instead of "cats"). If we stick to the original terms, this premise is factually false, but we are testing logical validity. Let's use a different set for B to make the premises plausible for a counterexample:
    *   Let A = "dogs"
    *   Let B = "fish"
    *   Let C = "mammals"

*   Premise 1: No dogs are fish. (True: ∀x (Dog(x) → ¬Fish(x)))
*   Premise 2: No fish are mammals. (True: ∀x (Fish(x) → ¬Mammal(x)))

*   Conclusion: No dogs are mammals. (False: ∀x (Dog(x) → ¬Mammal(x)))

Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

    *   Let L(x) = x is an elevator

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "It is not the case that every single scientist is an engineer."
        *   ¬∀x (S(x) → E(x))
        *   This is equivalent to ∃x ¬(S(x) → E(x))
        *   Which is equivalent to ∃x (S(x) ∧ ¬E(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "Every scientist is an elevator."
        *   ∀x (S(x) → L(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "Some elevators are engineers."
        *   ∃x (L(x) ∧ E(x))

    *   Let T(x) = x is a table
    *   Let D(x) = x is a desk
    *   Let C(x) = x is a chair

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There is no table that is a desk."
        *   ¬∃x (T(x) ∧ D(x))
        *   Equivalent to: ∀x (T(x) → ¬D(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "No desks are chairs whatsoever."
        *   ¬∃x (D(x) ∧ C(x))
        *   Equivalent to: ∀x (D(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "Therefore, we can conclude that no chair is a table."
        *   ¬∃x (C(x) ∧ T(x))
        *   Equivalent to: ∀x (C(x) → ¬T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no humans that are animals. Every single doctor is not a human. Therefore, we can deduce that no doctors are animals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There are no humans that are animals. Every single doctor is not a human. Therefore, we can deduce that no doctors are animals.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let H = humans
    *   Let A = animals
    *   Let D = doctors

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There are no humans that are animals.
        ∀x (H(x) → ¬A(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** Every single doctor is not a human.
        ∀x (D(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Therefore, we can deduce that no doctors are animals.
        ∀x (D(x) → ¬A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        Let's assume the premises are true.
        *   From Premise 2: If someone is a doctor (D(x)), then they are not a human (¬H(x)).
        *   From Premise 1: If someone is a human (H(x)), then they are not an animal (¬A(x)).

        The conclusion states: If someone is a doctor (D(x)), then they are not an animal (¬A(x)).

        Let's trace the logic:
        1.  Assume D(x) is true (x is a doctor).
        2.  From Premise 2, we deduce ¬H(x) (x is not a human).
        3.  Now we know x is not a human. Premise 1 states H(x) → ¬A(x). This premise only tells us what happens if x *is* a human. If x is *not* a human (which we've deduced), the premise H(x) → ¬A(x) is true regardless of whether x is an animal or not. Knowing that x is not a human does not logically force x to be not an animal.

        Consider a counterexample where the premises are true but the conclusion is false:
        *   Let H = {ghosts}
        *   Let A = {dogs, cats, fish}
        *   Let D = {Dr. Smith, Dr. Jones, Fido (a dog)}

        *   **Premise 1 Check:** Are there any humans (ghosts) that are animals (dogs/cats/fish)? No. (TRUE)
        *   **Premise 2 Check:** Is every single doctor (Dr. Smith, Dr. Jones, Fido) not a human (ghost)? Yes, none of them are ghosts. (TRUE)
        *   **Conclusion Check:** Is it true that no doctors are animals? No, because Fido is a doctor and Fido is an animal. Therefore, the conclusion "no doctors are animals" is FALSE in this scenario.

        Since we found a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is true that all fish live in water. No birds are fish. Therefore, no bird lives in water.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** It is true that all fish live in water. No birds are fish. Therefore, no bird lives in water.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fish
    *   Let W(x) = x lives in water
    *   Let B(x) = x is a bird

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (F(x) → W(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → ¬F(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (B(x) → ¬W(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises state that all fish live in water, and no birds are fish. From this, we cannot logically conclude that no birds live in water. The fact that something is not a fish (like a bird) does not prevent it from living in water. For example, a duck is a bird, and a duck is not a fish, but a duck lives in water. In this scenario, both premises are true, but the conclusion ("no bird lives in water") is false for the duck.

### Response:

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x))

    *   Conclusion: ∃x (F(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single vegetable is not a fruit. A portion of the things that are croissants are fruits. The result of this is that some croissants are vegetables.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Every single vegetable is not a fruit. A portion of the things that are croissants are fruits. The result of this is that some croissants are vegetables.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let V(x) = x is a vegetable
    Let F(x) = x is a fruit
    Let C(x) = x is a croissant

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (V(x) → ¬F(x))
    Premise 2: ∃x (C(x) ∧ F(x))
    Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ V(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Reasoning:**
        Premise 1 states that no vegetable is a fruit (V and F are disjoint sets).
        Premise 2 states that there is at least one thing (let's call it 'k') that is both a croissant and a fruit. So, C(k) and F(k) are true.
        From Premise 1, since F(k) is true, it must be the case that ¬V(k) is true (i.e., 'k' is not a vegetable).
        Therefore, the specific croissant 'k' mentioned in Premise 2 is *not* a vegetable. This means that any croissant that is a fruit cannot be a vegetable.
        The premises do not provide any information to suggest that there must be *other* croissants (those that are not fruits) that *are* vegetables. It is entirely possible for all croissants that are fruits to not be vegetables, and for all croissants that are not fruits to also not be vegetables.
        For example, imagine:
        *   Vegetables = {Carrot}
        *   Fruits = {Apple, Orange}
        *   Croissants = {Apple, Plain Croissant}
        Premise 1: Carrot is not an Apple or Orange. (True)
        Premise 2: Apple is a Croissant and an Apple is a Fruit. (True)
        Conclusion: Some Croissants are Vegetables. (Is Apple a Carrot? No. Is Plain Croissant a Carrot? No. So, False)
        Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Dogs are in no way mammals. Anything that is a dog is a canine. There are some canines that are not mammals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Dogs are in no way mammals. Anything that is a dog is a canine. There are some canines that are not mammals.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let D = dogs
    *   Let M = mammals
    *   Let C = canines

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1 (Dogs are in no way mammals):** ∀x (D(x) → ¬M(x))
    *   **Premise 2 (Anything that is a dog is a canine):** ∀x (D(x) → C(x))
    *   **Conclusion (There are some canines that are not mammals):** ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
This syllogism commits the **existential fallacy**. Both premises are universal statements, while the conclusion is an existential (particular) statement. In modern predicate logic, universal statements do not imply the existence of their subjects.

Consider a scenario where the set of "dogs" (D) is empty.
*   If D is empty, then Premise 1 (∀x (D(x) → ¬M(x))) is vacuously true (because D(x) is always false).
*   If D is empty, then Premise 2 (∀x (D(x) → C(x))) is also vacuously true.
*   However, if D is empty, the conclusion (∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬M(x))) does not necessarily follow. For example, if all canines (C) were also mammals (M), then the conclusion would be false, even though the premises remain true.

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Input:
It is not the case that every novel is a short story. Every single thing that is a novel is a book. One can therefore conclude that some books are not short stories.

    Let S(x) = x is a short story
    Let B(x) = x is a book

    Premise 2: ∀x (N(x) → B(x))
    Conclusion: ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬S(x))

Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Response:

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything that is a animal is an idea. There are no ideas that are dogs. This necessarily suggests that some dogs are not animals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Anything that is a animal is an idea. There are no ideas that are dogs. This necessarily suggests that some dogs are not animals.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A(x) = x is an animal
    *   Let B(x) = x is an idea
    *   Let C(x) = x is a dog

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "Anything that is a animal is an idea."
        *   ∀x (A(x) → B(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "There are no ideas that are dogs."
        *   This means it's not the case that there exists something that is both an idea and a dog.
        *   ¬∃x (B(x) ∧ C(x))
        *   This is logically equivalent to: ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x)) (If something is an idea, then it is not a dog)
    *   **Conclusion:** "This necessarily suggests that some dogs are not animals."
        *   ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

    *   **Full Formulation:**
        *   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → B(x))
        *   Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))
        *   Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        From Premise 1 and Premise 2, we can derive a new universal statement using the hypothetical syllogism (transitivity of implication):
        1.  ∀x (A(x) → B(x))
        2.  ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))
        3.  Therefore, ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x)) (This means "No animals are dogs" or "All animals are not dogs").

        The derived statement (3) "No animals are dogs" is equivalent to "All dogs are not animals" (∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))).

        The conclusion given is "Some dogs are not animals" (∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))).

        In modern first-order logic, a universal statement like "All dogs are not animals" (∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x))) does not necessarily imply an existential statement like "Some dogs are not animals" (∃x (C(x) ∧ ¬A(x))). This is because universal statements do not carry existential import. If there are no dogs (i.e., the set C is empty), then "All dogs are not animals" would be vacuously true, but "Some dogs are not animals" would be false. Since the premises do not guarantee the existence of dogs, the conclusion does not necessarily follow.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Not a single person who is a poodle is a dog. Every single animal is a poodle. The conclusion that no animal is a dog is inescapable.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Not a single person who is a poodle is a dog. Every single animal is a poodle. The conclusion that no animal is a dog is inescapable.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A(x) = x is an animal
    *   Let P(x) = x is a poodle
    *   Let D(x) = x is a dog
    *   Let Person(x) = x is a person

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Not a single person who is a poodle is a dog.
        ∀x ((Person(x) ∧ P(x)) → ¬D(x))
    *   Premise 2: Every single animal is a poodle.
        ∀x (A(x) → P(x))
    *   Conclusion: No animal is a dog.
        ∀x (A(x) → ¬D(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    To show the syllogism is invalid, we need to find a scenario where both premises are true, but the conclusion is false.

    *   Assume the conclusion is false: This means there exists at least one 'x' such that x is an animal AND x is a dog. Let's call this 'a'. So, A(a) ∧ D(a) is true.

    *   From Premise 2: "Every single animal is a poodle."
        Since A(a) is true, it follows that P(a) is true. So, 'a' is a poodle.
        Now we know: A(a), D(a), and P(a) are all true for 'a'.

    *   Now let's check Premise 1: "Not a single person who is a poodle is a dog."
        This translates to: For any x, if x is a person AND x is a poodle, then x is NOT a dog.
        Consider our 'a'. We know P(a) is true and D(a) is true.
        For Premise 1 to be true, it must hold for 'a'.
        The implication is ((Person(a) ∧ P(a)) → ¬D(a)).
        Since D(a) is true, ¬D(a) is false.
        For the implication to be true when the consequent is false, the antecedent must also be false.
        So, (Person(a) ∧ P(a)) must be false.
        Since P(a) is true, this means Person(a) must be false.

    *   **Counterexample:**
        Imagine an animal 'a' that is a dog, and by Premise 2, is also a poodle. Furthermore, imagine this 'a' is *not* a person (e.g., a typical dog).
        1.  Premise 1: ((Person(a) ∧ P(a)) → ¬D(a)) becomes ((False ∧ True) → False), which simplifies to (False → False), which is TRUE. (Premise 1 holds).
        2.  Premise 2: (A(a) → P(a)) becomes (True → True), which is TRUE. (Premise 2 holds).
        3.  Conclusion: (A(a) → ¬D(a)) becomes (True → False), which is FALSE. (Conclusion is false).

    Since we can construct a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid. The "person" qualifier in the first premise prevents a direct logical link to the conclusion about "animals" in general.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
No animal that is a dog is a bird. Some of the animals known as beagles are dogs. As a result, some beagles are not birds.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: No animal that is a dog is a bird. Some of the animals known as beagles are dogs. As a result, some beagles are not birds.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = beagles
    Let B = dogs
    Let C = birds

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (B(x) → ¬C(x))
    Premise 2: ∃x (A(x) ∧ B(x))
    Conclusion: ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬C(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Each and every kitchen is a room. Each and every kitchen is a place for cooking. This implies that some places for cooking are rooms.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Each and every kitchen is a room. Each and every kitchen is a place for cooking. This implies that some places for cooking are rooms.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let K = kitchens
    *   Let R = rooms
    *   Let C = places for cooking

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (K(x) → R(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (K(x) → C(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ R(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation for Invalidity:**
In modern first-order logic, universal statements (like "All K are R") do not carry existential import; they do not imply that K(x) is true for any x. If we consider a scenario where there are no kitchens (i.e., K(x) is false for all x), then both premises become vacuously true:
*   Premise 1: ∀x (False → R(x)) is true.
*   Premise 2: ∀x (False → C(x)) is true.

However, the conclusion "∃x (C(x) ∧ R(x))" (Some places for cooking are rooms) could be false in such a scenario. For example, if there are no kitchens, and the only "places for cooking" are outdoor grills (not rooms), and the only "rooms" are bedrooms (not places for cooking), then the conclusion would be false. Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

Let C = cars

Premise 2: ∃x (C(x) ∧ M(x))
Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ A(x))

3. Final Answer: INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no numbers which are both even and odd. A few numbers that are odd are also divisible by two. Not all numbers that are divisible by two are even.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no numbers which are both even and odd. A few numbers that are odd are also divisible by two. Not all numbers that are divisible by two are even.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let E(x) = x is even
    *   Let O(x) = x is odd
    *   Let D(x) = x is divisible by two

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There are no numbers which are both even and odd."
        *   ∀x ¬(E(x) ∧ O(x))
        *   (This is logically equivalent to ∀x (O(x) → ¬E(x)) or ∀x (E(x) → ¬O(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** "A few numbers that are odd are also divisible by two."
        *   ∃x (O(x) ∧ D(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "Not all numbers that are divisible by two are even."
        *   ¬∀x (D(x) → E(x))
        *   (This is logically equivalent to ∃x ¬(D(x) → E(x)), which simplifies to ∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬E(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
Let's assume the premises are true.
From Premise 2, we know there exists at least one number, let's call it 'k', such that O(k) and D(k) are both true.
From Premise 1, we know that for any number, it's not both even and odd. This implies that if a number is odd, it cannot be even (i.e., O(x) → ¬E(x)).
Since O(k) is true (from Premise 2) and O(k) → ¬E(k) is true (from Premise 1), by Modus Ponens, we can conclude that ¬E(k) is true.
So, for this number 'k', we have:
*   D(k) (from Premise 2)
*   ¬E(k) (derived from Premise 1 and O(k))
Combining these, we find that D(k) ∧ ¬E(k) is true for this number 'k'.
Since such a 'k' exists, the conclusion ∃x (D(x) ∧ ¬E(x)) necessarily follows. Therefore, the syllogism is logically valid. (Note: The factual truth of the premises in standard mathematics is irrelevant to the logical validity of the argument's structure.)

3. Final Answer: VALID

    *   Let F(x) = x is a feline
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mammal

    *   Premise 1: ∀x (T(x) → F(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
All cars are a type of vehicle. No animal is a car. Therefore, no animal can be a vehicle.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** All cars are a type of vehicle. No animal is a car. Therefore, no animal can be a vehicle.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C = cars
    *   Let V = vehicles
    *   Let A = animals

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (C(x) → V(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (A(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (A(x) → ¬V(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    **Explanation of Invalidity:**
    This syllogism is invalid because the premises do not guarantee the conclusion. It's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false.

    Consider a counterexample: Let 'x' be a **horse**.
    *   **C(horse)** (A horse is a car) is **False**.
    *   **V(horse)** (A horse is a vehicle) can be considered **True** (e.g., as a means of transport or pulling a carriage).
    *   **A(horse)** (A horse is an animal) is **True**.

    Now, let's check the premises and conclusion with 'x' being a horse:
    *   **Premise 1: C(horse) → V(horse)**
        *   (False → True) is **True**. (If a horse is a car, then it's a vehicle. Since a horse is not a car, the implication holds.)
    *   **Premise 2: A(horse) → ¬C(horse)**
        *   (True → ¬False) is **True**. (If a horse is an animal, then it's not a car. This is true.)

    *   **Conclusion: A(horse) → ¬V(horse)**
        *   (True → ¬True) is **False**. (If a horse is an animal, then it's not a vehicle. This is false, as a horse is an animal and can be a vehicle.)

    Since both premises are true for 'x' being a horse, but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is true that some students are athletes. There are some tall people which are athletes. It logically follows that some tall people are not students.

### Response:
ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let S = students
    *   Let A = athletes
    *   Let T = tall people

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∃x (S(x) ∧ A(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∃x (T(x) ∧ A(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬S(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        This syllogism is invalid because the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises. It is possible for both premises to be true while the conclusion is false.
        Consider a scenario where:
        *   All tall people are students. (This makes the conclusion "some tall people are not students" false).
        *   There are some students who are athletes. (e.g., John is a student and an athlete).
        *   There are some tall people who are athletes. (e.g., Mary is tall and an athlete).

        If we assume "all tall people are students" (to make the conclusion false), then Mary (who is tall and an athlete) must also be a student. In this case, Mary is a student, tall, and an athlete. John is a student and an athlete. Both premises are true in this scenario, but the conclusion is false. Therefore, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every object that orbits a star is a planet. Anything that is a gas giant is an object that is a planet. Therefore, there are no gas giants that do not orbit a star.

### Response:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let O(x) = x is an object that orbits a star
    *   Let P(x) = x is a planet
    *   Let G(x) = x is a gas giant

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (O(x) → P(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (G(x) → P(x))
    *   Conclusion: ¬∃x (G(x) ∧ ¬O(x)) which is equivalent to ∀x (G(x) → O(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anything that is a vegetable is a plant. There is a subset of plants that are fruits. A number of vegetables are not fruits.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let V(x) = x is a vegetable
    *   Let P(x) = x is a plant
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fruit

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "Anything that is a vegetable is a plant."
        ∀x (V(x) → P(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "There is a subset of plants that are fruits." (This implies all fruits are plants.)
        ∀x (F(x) → P(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "A number of vegetables are not fruits." (This means some vegetables are not fruits.)
        ∃x (V(x) ∧ ¬F(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises state that all vegetables are plants, and all fruits are plants. Both vegetables and fruits are subsets of plants. However, the premises do not provide any information about the relationship *between* vegetables and fruits.

Consider a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false:
*   Let P = {apple, banana, carrot, potato} (all plants)
*   Let V = {apple, banana} (all vegetables are plants)
*   Let F = {apple, banana} (all fruits are plants)

In this scenario:
*   Premise 1: All vegetables (apples, bananas) are plants. (True)
*   Premise 2: All fruits (apples, bananas) are plants. (True)
*   Conclusion: A number of vegetables are not fruits. (False, because in this scenario, all vegetables are fruits).

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid. The conclusion does not necessarily follow from the given premises.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no mammals that are birds. Every single dog is a mammal. It is therefore the case that every dog is a bird.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = mammals
    Let B = birds
    Let C = dogs

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: There are no mammals that are birds. (∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x)))
    Premise 2: Every single dog is a mammal. (∀x (C(x) → A(x)))
    Conclusion: It is therefore the case that every dog is a bird. (∀x (C(x) → B(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:**
    INVALID

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Written works are in no way items with printed pages. Everything that is a book is an item with printed pages. Books are not written works.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Written works are in no way items with printed pages. Everything that is a book is an item with printed pages. Books are not written works.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let A = written works
    Let B = items with printed pages
    Let C = books

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬B(x)) (No written works are items with printed pages)
    Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → B(x)) (All books are items with printed pages)
    Conclusion: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x)) (No books are written works)

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
The set of artists contains no people who conduct systematic research. Every single scientist is a person who conducts systematic research. Artists are not scientists.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: The set of artists contains no people who conduct systematic research. Every single scientist is a person who conducts systematic research. Artists are not scientists.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A(x) = x is an artist
    *   Let R(x) = x conducts systematic research
    *   Let S(x) = x is a scientist

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → ¬R(x)) (If someone is an artist, they do not conduct systematic research)
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (S(x) → R(x)) (If someone is a scientist, they conduct systematic research)
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (A(x) → ¬S(x)) (If someone is an artist, they are not a scientist)

3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is no celestial body that is a thing in the sky. All planets are things in the sky. The implication is that a number of planets are not celestial bodies.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C(x) = x is a celestial body
    *   Let S(x) = x is a thing in the sky
    *   Let P(x) = x is a planet

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There is no celestial body that is a thing in the sky.
        ∀x (C(x) → ¬S(x))
        (Alternatively, ¬∃x (C(x) ∧ S(x)))
    *   **Premise 2:** All planets are things in the sky.
        ∀x (P(x) → S(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** The implication is that a number of planets are not celestial bodies.
        ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))

    **Derivation Check:**
    From Premise 1: ∀x (C(x) → ¬S(x))
    By contraposition, this is equivalent to: ∀x (S(x) → ¬C(x)) (If something is a thing in the sky, then it is not a celestial body).

    Now, combine this with Premise 2:
    1.  ∀x (P(x) → S(x)) (Premise 2: All planets are things in the sky)
    2.  ∀x (S(x) → ¬C(x)) (Derived from Premise 1)

    By hypothetical syllogism (transitivity of implication), we can conclude:
    ∀x (P(x) → ¬C(x)) (All planets are not celestial bodies, or No planet is a celestial body).

    The derived conclusion is a universal statement: "No planet is a celestial body."
    The given conclusion is an existential statement: "Some planets are not celestial bodies" (∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))).

    In modern predicate logic, a universal statement (like ∀x (P(x) → ¬C(x))) does not necessarily imply an existential statement (like ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x))). If the set of planets (P) were empty, then ∀x (P(x) → ¬C(x)) would be vacuously true, but ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬C(x)) would be false. Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid without an additional premise asserting the existence of planets.

3.  **Final Answer:**
    INVALID

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Anyone who is a teacher is an educator. It is impossible for a student to be an educator. Consequently, every student is a teacher.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Anyone who is a teacher is an educator. It is impossible for a student to be an educator. Consequently, every student is a teacher.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let T = is a teacher
    *   Let E = is an educator
    *   Let S = is a student

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: Anyone who is a teacher is an educator.
        ∀x (T(x) → E(x))
    *   Premise 2: It is impossible for a student to be an educator. (This means no student is an educator, or if someone is a student, they are not an educator.)
        ∀x (S(x) → ¬E(x))
    *   Conclusion: Consequently, every student is a teacher.
        ∀x (S(x) → T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Explanation of Invalidity:**
        Let's assume the premises are true.
        From Premise 1: If someone is a teacher, they are an educator (T → E). The contrapositive is: If someone is *not* an educator, they are *not* a teacher (¬E → ¬T).
        From Premise 2: If someone is a student, they are *not* an educator (S → ¬E).

        Now, let's try to derive the conclusion (S → T):
        If we take an arbitrary individual 'a' who is a student (S(a)), then from Premise 2, we know that 'a' is not an educator (¬E(a)).
        Since 'a' is not an educator (¬E(a)), and from the contrapositive of Premise 1 (¬E → ¬T), it follows that 'a' is not a teacher (¬T(a)).

        Therefore, the premises lead to the conclusion: If someone is a student, then they are *not* a teacher (∀x (S(x) → ¬T(x))). This directly contradicts the given conclusion (∀x (S(x) → T(x))). Thus, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no squares which are round. There are no circles which are squares. It follows, then, that some circles are square.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no squares which are round. There are no circles which are squares. It follows, then, that some circles are square.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:** Let S = squares, R = round things, C = circles
2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (S(x) → ¬R(x)) (or equivalently, ¬∃x (S(x) ∧ R(x)))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (C(x) → ¬S(x)) (or equivalently, ¬∃x (C(x) ∧ S(x)))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ S(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
A few of the insects are beetles. Every beetle is an arthropod. As a result, some arthropods are insects.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: A few of the insects are beetles. Every beetle is an arthropod. As a result, some arthropods are insects.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:** Let I = insects, B = beetles, A = arthropods
2.  **Logical Formulation:** Premise 1: ∃x (I(x) ∧ B(x)). Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → A(x)). Conclusion: ∃x (A(x) ∧ I(x))
3.  **Final Answer:** VALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every cat is a mammal. The set of dolphins contains no cats. Every single dolphin is a mammal.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Every cat is a mammal. The set of dolphins contains no cats. Every single dolphin is a mammal.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C(x) = x is a cat
    *   Let M(x) = x is a mammal
    *   Let D(x) = x is a dolphin

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (C(x) → M(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (D(x) → ¬C(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (D(x) → M(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises state that all cats are mammals, and no dolphins are cats. This does not logically guarantee that all dolphins are mammals. It's possible for dolphins to be neither cats nor mammals, while still satisfying both premises.

Consider a counterexample:
*   Let C = {cats}
*   Let M = {mammals}
*   Let D = {fish} (In this hypothetical scenario, dolphins are fish, not mammals)

1.  Every cat is a mammal: True (All members of C are in M).
2.  The set of dolphins contains no cats: True (No members of D are in C).
3.  Therefore, every single dolphin is a mammal: False (Members of D are fish, not mammals).

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
All plants are in need of sunlight. All trees are, by definition, plants. At least one tree needs sunlight.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** All plants are in need of sunlight. All trees are, by definition, plants. At least one tree needs sunlight.

**ANALYSIS:**
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let P(x) = x is a plant
    *   Let S(x) = x is in need of sunlight
    *   Let T(x) = x is a tree

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (P(x) → S(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (T(x) → P(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (T(x) ∧ S(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
From Premise 2 (∀x (T(x) → P(x))) and Premise 1 (∀x (P(x) → S(x))), we can logically deduce, through the transitivity of implication, that ∀x (T(x) → S(x)). This means "All trees need sunlight."

However, in modern symbolic logic, a universal affirmative statement (like "All trees need sunlight") does not necessarily imply an existential statement (like "At least one tree needs sunlight"). If there were no trees in existence (i.e., the set of trees is empty), then "All trees need sunlight" would still be true (because the antecedent T(x) would always be false, making T(x) → S(x) always true). But in such a scenario, "At least one tree needs sunlight" would be false.

Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false (in a world where no trees exist), the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are some animals that are not mammals. Everything that is a dog is a mammal. Thus, every dog is an animal.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = animals
    *   Let B = mammals
    *   Let C = dogs

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** There are some animals that are not mammals.
        ∃x (A(x) ∧ ¬B(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** Everything that is a dog is a mammal.
        ∀x (C(x) → B(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** Thus, every dog is an animal.
        ∀x (C(x) → A(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There is no fire which is hot. All of the bonfires are fire. Thus, there exist some bonfires which are not hot.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** There is no fire which is hot. All of the bonfires are fire. Thus, there exist some bonfires which are not hot.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let F(x) = x is fire
    *   Let H(x) = x is hot
    *   Let B(x) = x is a bonfire

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There is no fire which is hot."
        This means for all x, if x is fire, then x is not hot.
        Symbolically: ∀x (F(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "All of the bonfires are fire."
        This means for all x, if x is a bonfire, then x is fire.
        Symbolically: ∀x (B(x) → F(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** "Thus, there exist some bonfires which are not hot."
        This means there is at least one x such that x is a bonfire AND x is not hot.
        Symbolically: ∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬H(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**

From Premise 1 (∀x (F(x) → ¬H(x))) and Premise 2 (∀x (B(x) → F(x))), we can logically deduce, through the transitivity of implication (or hypothetical syllogism), that:
∀x (B(x) → ¬H(x))
This means "All bonfires are not hot" or "No bonfire is hot."

The conclusion is "There exist some bonfires which are not hot" (∃x (B(x) ∧ ¬H(x))).

In modern symbolic logic, a universal statement (like "All B are not H") does not imply an existential statement (like "Some B are not H") unless the existence of the subject (B) is explicitly stated or guaranteed by another premise.

Consider a scenario where the premises are true, but the conclusion is false:
*   **Assume there are no bonfires.** (i.e., B(x) is false for all x).
    *   **Premise 1:** "There is no fire which is hot." (True)
    *   **Premise 2:** "All of the bonfires are fire." (This is vacuously true, as there are no bonfires to contradict the statement).
    *   **Conclusion:** "There exist some bonfires which are not hot." (This is false, because there are no bonfires at all).

Since it's possible for the premises to be true while the conclusion is false, the syllogism is **INVALID**.

SYLLOGISM: There are no dogs who are cats. A few dogs are pets. This implies that some pets are cats.

    *   Let C = cats
    *   Let P = pets

    *   Conclusion: ∃x (P(x) ∧ C(x))

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no tables that are living things. It is not true that any plant is a table. It is the case that some plants are tables.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no tables that are living things. It is not true that any plant is a table. It is the case that some plants are tables.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let T = tables
    *   Let L = living things
    *   Let P = plants

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** "There are no tables that are living things."
        *   ∀x (T(x) → ¬L(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** "It is not true that any plant is a table."
        *   ¬∀x (P(x) → T(x))
        *   (This is logically equivalent to ∃x (P(x) ∧ ¬T(x)), meaning "Some plants are not tables.")
    *   **Conclusion:** "It is the case that some plants are tables."
        *   ∃x (P(x) ∧ T(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every nurse is a healthcare professional. Not a single teacher is a healthcare professional. A part of the population of teachers are not nurses.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Every nurse is a healthcare professional. Not a single teacher is a healthcare professional. A part of the population of teachers are not nurses.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let N = nurses
    *   Let H = healthcare professionals
    *   Let T = teachers

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (N(x) → H(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (T(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬N(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

    *   **Derivation from Premises:**
        1.  From Premise 1: ∀x (N(x) → H(x))
        2.  The contrapositive of Premise 1 is: ∀x (¬H(x) → ¬N(x))
        3.  From Premise 2: ∀x (T(x) → ¬H(x))
        4.  Combining (3) and (2) using hypothetical syllogism: ∀x (T(x) → ¬N(x)). This means "No teacher is a nurse."

    *   **Validity Check:**
        The derived statement is ∀x (T(x) → ¬N(x)) ("No teacher is a nurse").
        The conclusion is ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬N(x)) ("Some teachers are not nurses").

        In modern predicate logic, a universal statement (like "No teacher is a nurse") does not imply an existential statement (like "Some teachers are not nurses") if the subject class (teachers) might be empty. If the set of teachers (T) is empty, then:
        *   The derived statement ∀x (T(x) → ¬N(x)) is vacuously true.
        *   The conclusion ∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬N(x)) is false (because there are no teachers to be not nurses).

        Since the premises can be true while the conclusion is false (in the scenario where there are no teachers), the syllogism is **INVALID**. The existence of teachers is not guaranteed by the premises, even though the conclusion asserts it.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every bicycle is a vehicle. Every bicycle is an item that has wheels. Therefore, this implies that some items with wheels are vehicles.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Every bicycle is a vehicle. Every bicycle is an item that has wheels. Therefore, this implies that some items with wheels are vehicles.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let A = bicycles
    *   Let B = vehicles
    *   Let C = items that have wheels

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (A(x) → B(x))
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (A(x) → C(x))
    *   Conclusion: ∃x (C(x) ∧ B(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
In modern symbolic logic, universal statements (like "Every A is B") do not inherently imply the existence of the subject (A). If we assume that there are no bicycles (i.e., the set A is empty), then both premises become vacuously true (because the antecedent A(x) is always false). However, if there are no bicycles, it does not necessarily follow that "some items with wheels are vehicles." For example, imagine a world where there are no bicycles, all vehicles are cars, and all items with wheels are unicycles. In this scenario, the premises would be true, but the conclusion (that some items with wheels are vehicles) would be false, as cars and unicycles are distinct categories. Therefore, the conclusion does not necessarily follow from the premises.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
There are no reptiles that are humans. It is also true that no ant is a reptile. It follows that no ant is a human.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: There are no reptiles that are humans. It is also true that no ant is a reptile. It follows that no ant is a human.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let R = reptiles
    *   Let H = humans
    *   Let A = ants

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: "There are no reptiles that are humans." This means if something is a reptile, it is not a human.
        *   ∀x (R(x) → ¬H(x))
    *   Premise 2: "No ant is a reptile." This means if something is an ant, it is not a reptile.
        *   ∀x (A(x) → ¬R(x))
    *   Conclusion: "No ant is a human." This means if something is an ant, it is not a human.
        *   ∀x (A(x) → ¬H(x))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
Let's assume the premises are true and try to find a scenario where the conclusion is false.
If the conclusion is false, then there must exist at least one individual 'k' such that 'k' is an ant AND 'k' is a human. So, A(k) is true and H(k) is true.

Now let's check if this scenario is consistent with the premises:
*   **Premise 1: R(k) → ¬H(k)**
    Since H(k) is true, ¬H(k) is false. For the implication R(k) → ¬H(k) to be true (as the premise states), R(k) must be false. So, 'k' cannot be a reptile (¬R(k)).
*   **Premise 2: A(k) → ¬R(k)**
    Since A(k) is true, and we've established that ¬R(k) must be true for Premise 1 to hold, this premise becomes True → True, which is true.

So, we can construct a counterexample: Imagine an individual 'k' who is an ant (A(k)), is a human (H(k)), but is not a reptile (¬R(k)).
In this scenario:
*   Premise 1 (R(k) → ¬H(k)) becomes (False → False), which is True.
*   Premise 2 (A(k) → ¬R(k)) becomes (True → True), which is True.
*   Conclusion (A(k) → ¬H(k)) becomes (True → False), which is False.

Since we found a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Every single animal that is a cat is a vertebrate. Some fish are classified as vertebrates. As a result, not all fish are cats.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** Every single animal that is a cat is a vertebrate. Some fish are classified as vertebrates. As a result, not all fish are cats.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C(x) = x is a cat
    *   Let V(x) = x is a vertebrate
    *   Let F(x) = x is a fish

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   **Premise 1:** ∀x (C(x) → V(x))
    *   **Premise 2:** ∃x (F(x) ∧ V(x))
    *   **Conclusion:** ¬∀x (F(x) → C(x))  (which is equivalent to ∃x (F(x) ∧ ¬C(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Validity:**
To check for validity, we assume the premises are true and see if the conclusion *must* also be true. If we can find a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

Let's assume the premises are true:
*   P1: All cats are vertebrates. (C ⊆ V)
*   P2: Some fish are vertebrates. (F ∩ V ≠ ∅)

Now, let's assume the *negation* of the conclusion is true:
*   ¬Conclusion: All fish are cats. (∀x (F(x) → C(x)), or F ⊆ C)

If we can find a model where P1, P2, and ¬Conclusion are all true, then the original syllogism is invalid.

Consider a universe where there is only one creature, "Nemo," and Nemo is a fish, a cat, and a vertebrate.
*   C(Nemo) = True
*   V(Nemo) = True
*   F(Nemo) = True

Let's check the statements:
1.  **P1: ∀x (C(x) → V(x))**
    *   Is it true that if Nemo is a cat, then Nemo is a vertebrate? (True → True) = True. So P1 is true.
2.  **P2: ∃x (F(x) ∧ V(x))**
    *   Is it true that there exists an x such that x is a fish AND x is a vertebrate? Yes, Nemo is a fish and Nemo is a vertebrate. (True ∧ True) = True. So P2 is true.
3.  **¬Conclusion: ∀x (F(x) → C(x))**
    *   Is it true that if Nemo is a fish, then Nemo is a cat? (True → True) = True. So the negation of the conclusion is true.

Since we found a scenario (a model) where both premises are true, but the negation of the conclusion is also true (meaning the original conclusion is false), the syllogism is **INVALID**. The premises do not guarantee that not all fish are cats; it's possible for all fish to be cats while the premises hold.

================================================================================

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
A car is never an airplane. It is also the case that no boat is an airplane. Consequently, all boats are cars.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

**SYLLOGISM:** A car is never an airplane. It is also the case that no boat is an airplane. Consequently, all boats are cars.

**ANALYSIS:**

1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    *   Let C(x) = x is a car
    *   Let A(x) = x is an airplane
    *   Let B(x) = x is a boat

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    *   Premise 1: ∀x (C(x) → ¬A(x)) (A car is never an airplane)
    *   Premise 2: ∀x (B(x) → ¬A(x)) (No boat is an airplane)
    *   Conclusion: ∀x (B(x) → C(x)) (All boats are cars)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
Nothing that is a plant is a mammal. A portion of trees are to be found among the plants. Thus, there are no trees that are also mammals.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: Nothing that is a plant is a mammal. A portion of trees are to be found among the plants. Thus, there are no trees that are also mammals.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let P = plants
    Let M = mammals
    Let T = trees

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (P(x) → ¬M(x)) (or ¬∃x (P(x) ∧ M(x)))
    Premise 2: ∃x (T(x) ∧ P(x))
    Conclusion: ∀x (T(x) → ¬M(x)) (or ¬∃x (T(x) ∧ M(x)))

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
From Premise 2, we know there exists at least one individual, let's call it 'a', such that 'a' is a tree and 'a' is a plant (T(a) ∧ P(a)).
From Premise 1, since 'a' is a plant (P(a)), it must not be a mammal (¬M(a)).
So, we can deduce that there exists at least one individual 'a' such that 'a' is a tree and 'a' is not a mammal (∃x (T(x) ∧ ¬M(x))).
However, the conclusion states that *all* trees are not mammals (∀x (T(x) → ¬M(x))). The fact that *some* trees are not mammals does not logically guarantee that *all* trees are not mammals. There could be other trees (not among the "portion of trees that are plants") that *are* mammals.

**Counterexample:**
Imagine a world where:
*   **Plants (P):** Oak trees, Roses
*   **Mammals (M):** Cats, Dogs, **"Tree-Mammals"** (a hypothetical creature that is a mammal and a tree, but not a plant)
*   **Trees (T):** Oak trees, **"Tree-Mammals"**

1.  **Premise 1: Nothing that is a plant is a mammal.** (True: Oak trees and Roses are not mammals. "Tree-Mammals" are mammals but not plants.)
2.  **Premise 2: A portion of trees are to be found among the plants.** (True: Oak trees are both trees and plants.)
3.  **Conclusion: Thus, there are no trees that are also mammals.** (False: "Tree-Mammals" are trees and are also mammals.)

Since we can construct a scenario where the premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.

### Instruction:
Analyze the following syllogism and determine its validity based purely on its structure.

### Input:
It is the case that no ghost is immortal. There is not a single human that is a ghost. It follows that no human is immortal.

### Response:
Here's the analysis of the syllogism:

SYLLOGISM: It is the case that no ghost is immortal. There is not a single human that is a ghost. It follows that no human is immortal.

ANALYSIS:
1.  **Symbolic Abstraction:**
    Let G = ghosts
    Let I = immortal beings
    Let H = humans

2.  **Logical Formulation:**
    Premise 1: ∀x (G(x) → ¬I(x)) (No ghost is immortal)
    Premise 2: ∀x (H(x) → ¬G(x)) (No human is a ghost)
    Conclusion: ∀x (H(x) → ¬I(x)) (No human is immortal)

3.  **Final Answer:** INVALID

**Explanation of Invalidity:**
The premises state that the set of ghosts (G) and the set of immortal beings (I) are disjoint (P1). They also state that the set of humans (H) and the set of ghosts (G) are disjoint (P2). However, these premises do not establish a necessary disjoint relationship between humans (H) and immortal beings (I). It is possible for humans to be immortal without contradicting either premise.

Consider a counterexample:
Imagine a universe where:
*   Ghosts (G) are defined as non-corporeal entities.
*   Immortal beings (I) include gods and vampires.
*   Humans (H) are mortal, *except* for a specific human, "Alice," who became a god and is therefore immortal.

In this scenario:
*   **Premise 1: No ghost is immortal.** (True, non-corporeal entities are not gods or vampires).
*   **Premise 2: No human is a ghost.** (True, humans are not non-corporeal entities).
*   **Conclusion: No human is immortal.** (False, because Alice is a human and Alice is immortal).

Since we can construct a scenario where both premises are true but the conclusion is false, the syllogism is invalid.
